Micro-Intervention Technology for promoting 
Healthy Grocery Shopping Habits in Supermarkets 
Master Thesis 
AUTHOR 
Kristine Amalie Ohlen – s165713 
(KU student, rvb233) 
 
SUPERVISORS 
Per Bækgaard 
Dan Roland Persson 
20th December 2021 
==PAGE:2==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
 
Abstract 
For the millions of type 2 diabetics worldwide, micro-intervention technologies promoting a 
healthy lifestyle can be useful for diabetes management. In connection with a PhD project and 
based on a preceding special course, the problem statement for this project was: How should a 
micro-intervention technology be designed in order to promote healthy grocery shopping habits 
in supermarkets for type 2 diabetics? Initially, this was investigated by four series of searches. 
These resulted in six categories being habit of type 2 diabetics (2 articles), challenges of type 2 
diabetics (7 articles), nudging and motivation interventions (11 articles), existing mobile 
applications (61 apps), design and use of technologies (3 articles), and related concepts (4 
concepts). 
After these, the Design Thinking framework was used for designing a technology. In the first phase 
Emphasize, a focus group with two type 2 diabetics was conducted, which in the following Define 
phase was analyzed by the Abductive Grounded Theory process. The results were discussed using 
the identified literature in this project and the special course, as well as characteristics of 
functionalities. Finally, the point-of-view (POV) was created. It states that type 2 diabetics are 
different and responsible for their own health, and it contains the three needs personalization, 
nutritional information, and knowledge of how different foods affect the blood glucose. 
The last three phases Ideate, Prototype, and Test were incorporated in a co-design workshop. In 
this, a brainwalking involving three type 2 diabetics and a designer constituted the ideation and 
prototyping, while the testing occurred via three thinking aloud tests with the type 2 diabetics. The 
results were analyzed with affinity diagramming and severity ratings. Based on this and the POV, 
another Prototype phase was performed, where a new iteration of the prototype from the special 
course resulted in the interactive prototype of the mobile application MyGlucoseShopping. In a 
Test phase iteration, this was reviewed by an UX expert, who identified four usability problems. 
Therefore, a new iteration of the prototype should be created and tested. As well, more iterations 
of the Design Thinking phases are needed to ensure that the designed micro-intervention 
technology is promoting healthy grocery shopping habits in supermarkets for type 2 diabetics.

Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021

1. Introduction 
Worldwide, 537 million adults are estimated to be living with diabetes in 2021, and the prediction 
is that the number will be even higher in the future. The majority being more than 90% of the 
diabetes cases are type 2 diabetes (International Diabetes Federation, 2021). Type 2 diabetes is a 
chronic disease with an increased the blood glucose level due to a reduced production of insulin. 
This reduced production is caused by insulin resistance, which the pancreas isn’t capable of 
compensating for. With type 2 diabetes follows an increased risk of complications such as 
cardiovascular diseases as well as complications in eyes, kidneys, and feet (Almdal, 2021). These 
complications can be delayed or avoided with diabetes management, where a healthy lifestyle is 
central to promote (International Diabetes Federation, 2021). This promotion could be possible 
with micro-intervention technologies, which are providing motivation through interventions e.g. 
knowledge or suggestions adapted to the individual and the context (Matthisson, 2020).  
In connection with the PhD project Personalized Micro-Intervention Technology for Diabetes Self-
Management by Dan Roland Persson and based on the preceding special course Micro-
Intervention Technology for promoting Healthier Shopping Habits, this project is investigating the 
following problem statement (Matthisson, 2020): 
How should a micro-intervention technology be designed in order to promote healthy grocery 
shopping habits in supermarkets for type 2 diabetics? 
This will be investigated through initial searches covering grocery shopping habits and challenges 
of type 2 diabetics, existing mobile applications promoting healthy grocery shopping, as well as 
concepts relevant in this context. Following these searches, Design Thinking will be used as 
framework for designing a technology (The Interaction Design Foundation, n.d.). The empathizing 
will occur through a focus group with type 2 diabetics, which in the following Define phase will 
be analyzed via the Abductive Grounded Theory process. The result will be a defined challenge 
including concrete insights about and needs for type 2 diabetics. Afterwards, a co-design workshop 
involving ideation, prototyping, and testing will be performed, and the results will be analyzed. 
On the basis of this and the identified needs, another iteration of the Prototype and Test phase will 
be conducted resulting in an interactive prototype of a micro-intervention technology promoting 
healthy grocery shopping habits in supermarkets for people living with type 2 diabetes. 
==PAGE:6==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
2 
 
2. Initial searches 
Since it in the preceding and attached special course was evaluated that another round of searches 
would be necessary, four series of searches were conducted initially. These series were therefore 
the starting point for the whole project. All the series included literature searches, however the 
third series as well involved searches for existing mobile applications. For literature searches, it’s 
important to document decisions during the process, and a search protocol can be used for this 
documentation. A search protocol specifies the research questions for the searches, the sources, 
the search strategies, as well as the inclusion and exclusion criteria for the searches (Buus et al., 
2008). The search protocol for the four series of searches in this project are found in Appendix 1. 
In this, fourth research questions, which constitute the initial investigation of the problem 
statement, are: 
 
1. Which grocery shopping habits do type 2 diabetics have? 
2. Which challenges do type 2 diabetics experience related to healthy grocery shopping? 
3. What mobile applications exist to promote healthy grocery shopping in supermarkets? 
4. What do the related concepts empowerment, health literacy, and technology literacy 
cover? 
 
2.1 Grocery shopping Habits 
The first series of literature searches concerning the first research question from the search protocol 
was performed the 24th-25th of August 2021. This was conducted since it in the preceding special 
course was evaluated that knowledge about how type 2 diabetics do their grocery shopping could 
be useful. In this series, three literature searches were performed. The search protocol in Appendix 
1 specifies the inclusion and exclusion criteria for these searches as well as that the sources for 
these were the search engine Google Scholar or the database PubMed. The first of three searches 
was carried out with Google Scholar, and the search strategy was a block search consisting of three 
blocks. In this context, the two Boolean operators, “OR” and “AND”, were used. “OR” was used 
within the blocks, and “AND” was used to separate the blocks (Buus et al., 2008).  
==PAGE:7==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
3 
 
The theme of the first block is Patient group with free text, and the second block is the theme 
Behaviors/habits including truncated free text, which is done with an asterisk (*) at the end of a 
word. This indicates the shortest form of the word, therefore the search results include all the 
possible terminations of the truncated word (Buus et al., 2008). Finally, the theme for the third 
block is Publication type that included systematic reviews as free text. The block search is seen on 
figure 1.  
The initial result of the search was 19.300 articles. After this, an initial exclusion and two 
screenings of resp. title and abstract resulted in 5 included articles. These and the flowchart for the 
first search are seen on figure 1. One of these articles is about the dietary intake of type 2 diabetics 
and is placed in Appendix 2 concerning habits of type 2 diabetics (Burch et al., 2018). Another 
article describes the development of a mobile application for type 2 diabetes self-management 
(Goyal et al., 2016). This mobile application is described further in Appendix 5, which contains 
the relevant existing mobile applications identified. Two articles describe challenges for type 2 
diabetics, therefore they are found in Appendix 3 with challenges of type 2 diabetics (Campos et 
al., 2011)(Gardsten et al., 2018). The last included article is concerning the concept food literacy 
(Vaitkeviciute et al., 2015). This is described in Appendix 7 containing related concepts for this 
project.

The second literature search in the first series was as well performed with Google Scholar, but the 
search strategy was this time a standard search. The search line seen on figure 2 resulted initially 
in 17.900 articles. First, one exclusion and then two screenings were performed. This resulted in 
the number of included articles being 4. Figure 2 shows these and the flowchart of the second 
search. The articles by resp. S. G. Moore et al. 2018, Gupta et al. 2020, Gittelsohn et al. 2017, and 
Au et al. 2013 are all describing nudging interventions. Therefore, they are included in Appendix 
4 since it contains the identified motivation and nudging interventions.

The third literature search in the first series was conducted in PubMed. The search strategy was a 
block search including Boolean operators and three blocks like the first search (Buus et al., 2008). 
The first block has the theme Patient group and involves two MeSH-terms visualized with “[MH]”. 
The theme of the second block is Setting, which as well includes a MeSH-term, but this is truncated 
as previously described (Buus et al., 2008)(National Center for Biotechnology Information, 2021). 
All the MeSH-terms were identified before constructing this block search (National Center for 
Biotechnology Information, n.d.). Lastly, the theme for the third block is Behaviors/habits, and 
this involves truncated free text (Buus et al., 2008). Figure 3 shows the block search. 
By this search, only 11 articles were identified. This number was reduced to only 1 relevant article 
after screening first by title and then by abstract. The identified article by von Philipsborn et al. 
2020 describes nudging interventions, therefore it’s found in Appendix 4. Figure 3 contains the 
flowchart for the third search showing the identified article.

2.2 Grocery shopping Challenges 
On the 25th of August 2021, the second series of literature searches was conducted to answer the 
second research question found in the search protocol in Appendix 1 and in section 2. Like the 
first series, the purpose of this series was to understand how type 2 diabetics perform their grocery 
shopping, more specifically which challenges they experience in this context. The series consisted 
of three literature searches. The inclusion and exclusion criteria for these are described in the 
search protocol in Appendix 1, and it also contains the sources, which were either the search engine 
Google Scholar or the database PubMed. For the first search of the three searches, the search 
strategy was a block search constructed of two blocks and performed in PubMed. The Boolean 
operator “OR” was used within the block, and the blocks were separated with the Boolean operator 
“AND” (Buus et al., 2008). 
The theme of the first block is Patient group, where two MeSH-terms visualized with “[MH]” are 
included (Buus et al., 2008)(National Center for Biotechnology Information, 2021). The terms 
were found before creating the block search (National Center for Biotechnology Information, n.d.). 
The theme of the second block is Challenges involving truncated free text (Buus et al., 2008). The 
block search is seen on figure 4.  
A total of 5.328 articles were identified by this search. After two rounds of exclusions and then 
two screening rounds, 4 articles were included in the end. The flow of this search is visualized 
with a flowchart on figure 4. The articles by resp. Breuing et al. 2020, Alvarado et al. 2017, and 
Wilkinson et al. 2014 describe challenges for type 2 diabetics. These articles are therefore found 
in Appendix 3. The last identified article concerns motivating social network interventions and is 
placed in Appendix 4 (Spencer-Bonilla et al., 2017).  
 
==PAGE:15==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021

Figure 4: The flowchart of the first literature search in the second series. 
==PAGE:16==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen

The second literature search was like the first in the series a block search with Boolean operators 
and two blocks in PubMed (Buus et al., 2008). The first block has the same theme Patient group 
and includes the same two MeSH-terms (Buus et al., 2008)(National Center for Biotechnology 
Information, 2021). However, the second block has the theme Barriers instead of Challenges, 
which involves truncated free text (Buus et al., 2008). Figure 5 shows these details of the block 
search. 
The search resulted in 2.977 identified articles. However, after two exclusion rounds and two 
screenings of first title and then abstract, the number of articles was reduced to only 1 relevant 
article. In this article, Jain et al. 2020 identify challenges for type 2 diabetes self-management as 
well as diabetics’ preferences related to technology design and use. The challenges are found in 
Appendix 3, while the technology preferences are placed in Appendix 6 containing articles about 
design and use of technologies. Figure 5 shows a flowchart for the identification of this article.

The last of the three literature searches in the second series was conducted with Google Scholar. 
The search strategy was a standard search, and the search line is found on figure 6. Initially, the 
search resulted in 21.400 articles. Afterwards, two screening rounds of resp. title and abstract 
resulted in 2 articles being identified as relevant. Figure 6 visualizes the flowchart of how these 
articles are identified. The article by Stotz et al. 2021 describes challenges for type 2 diabetics’ 
self-management and interventions to overcome these challenges. The challenges are found in 
Appendix 3, and the interventions are placed in Appendix 4. The other identified article of Savoca 
& Miller 2001 concerns factors influencing the eating habits of type 2 diabetics and can be found 
in Appendix 2.

2.3 Existing mobile applications 
The third search series was conducted the 8th of September 2021 regarding the third research 
question in the search protocol seen in Appendix 1 and in section 2. The reason for this series was 
that it in the preceding special course was discovered that all existing mobile applications weren’t 
identified and that app stores weren’t searched. In total, ten different searches were performed in 
this series. The search protocol in Appendix 1 contains the inclusion and exclusion criteria for the 
searches and as well the sources for these. The sources were either the search engine Google, the 
website Apple, the online store Google Play, or the digital library of the Association for Computing 
Machinery (ACM). As well, the protocol specifies that the search strategy for all the searches was 
a standard search.  
The first search in the series was performed with Google using the search line shown on the figure 
7. Two of the resulting webpages on the first page of results were visited. The first webpage was 
Best Products, which was also identified in the preceding special course, and it contains 10 mobile 
applications (Pierre, 2020). These are described in Appendix 5. The second webpage visited was 
Digital Trends, which describes 8 mobile application (Lawrence, 2021). However, 4 of them were 
excluded. The other 4 mobile applications seen in Appendix 5 were included. The flowchart for 
the first search in the third series is seen on figure 7. 
 
==PAGE:22==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
18 
 
 
Figure 7: The flowchart of the first search in the third series. 
 
Likewise, the second search in the series was a Google search for relevant existing mobile 
applications, and figure 8 contains the search line used. Three webpages on the first page of search 
results were visited. The first webpage was Taste of Home, which contains 10 mobile applications 
(Tarlton, 2018). However, 5 of these applications were excluded and added to Appendix 5.  
The second visited webpage only concerns one mobile application namely Grosh, which is 
described in Appendix 5 (Grosh, n.d.). Finally, the third webpage visited was Good Housekeeping 
that describes 10 mobile applications (Picard, 2020). Due to exclusions, only 3 of these 
applications were included. These mobile applications are placed in Appendix 5. Figure 8 
visualizes the flowchart of the identification of the mobile applications from the second search in 
the third series.

Appendix 5.  
The second webpage Diabetes Strong shows 14 different mobile applications (Oerum, 2021). 
However, 11 of these were excluded, while the 3 included mobile applications are placed in 
Appendix 5. Lastly, the third relevant webpage was Everyday Health containing 16 mobile

of included mobile applications was therefore 7, which are found in Appendix 5. Figure 9 contains

Lastly, the ninth and tenth search in the third series were literature searches performed in the digital 
library of Association for Computing Machinery (ACM). This ninth search seen on figure 13 
initially resulted in 387.095 articles. Afterwards, a screening of title resulted in 36 exclusions, 
where one was due to the identification in the preceding special course. Therefore, the following 
screening of abstracts involved 4 articles, where 3 articles ended up being included. Figure 13 
contains the flowchart for the identification of these articles. 
The articles by resp. Bomfim et al. 2020 and Bomfim & Wallace 2018 both concern the gamified 
mobile application Pirate Bri’s Grocery Adventure, which is found in Appendix 5. Since the 
purpose of the application is to improve the food literacy in the context of grocery shopping, the

two articles as well define the concept food literacy, therefore they are placed in Appendix 7

The tenth search in the digital library of ACM gave 33.625 search results. The articles on the two 
first pages were screened by title, and the remaining articles were screened by abstract. In the end, 
3 articles were included. Figure 14 shows these articles and the flowchart of the tenth search. All 
these articles found in Appendix 4 describe a nudging or motivation intervention (DiCosola III & 
Neff, 2020)(Li et al., 2009)(Wan et al., 2017).

From the third series of searches, 60 relevant mobile applications are found. 59 of these 
applications are identified on webpages or in online stores, but 1 application is described in two 
articles. In total, 61 mobile applications are identified since one is found during the first series of 
literature searches. All these mobile applications are placed in Appendix 5, where they are related 
to 16 out of the 20 characteristics, which are categories of functionalities created based on the 
identified 28 mobile applications in the special course. The last 4 characteristics aren’t included 
since they aren’t present in the mobile applications identified in this project. However, new 
functionalities are identified and have extended some of the characteristics e.g., Personalization is 
extended with personalized meal plans, Access nutritional information now involves nutritional 
information of recipes, and Collection of recipes now contains the possibility to import recipes. 
Opposite, some functionalities in the mobile applications identified in this project don’t fit into the 
existing characteristics such as shareable recipes, a list of friendly and non-friendly diabetes foods, 
and graphs showing the effect of diet on the blood glucose level.  
Furthermore, the characteristic Support nutritional food choices is changed because it was

2.4 Empowerment and Literacy  
The 23rd of September 2021, the fourth series of literature searches was performed to answer the 
fourth research question found in the search protocol in Appendix 1 and in section 2. This series 
was conducted for two reasons. The first being that empowerment was found to be a concept 
related to a technology promoting healthy grocery shopping habits for type 2 diabetics in the 
preceding special course. The second being that the food literacy concept identified in the third 
series inspired the search for more literacies. In this series, two literature searches were conducted. 
The search protocol in Appendix 1 describes the inclusion and exclusion criteria for these and as 
well shows that the source for both searches was the database PubMed. Within this, the search 
strategy was a block search (Buus et al., 2008). 
These block searches consisted of two blocks. The Boolean operator “AND” was used for 
separating the two blocks, while the Boolean operator “OR” was used within the second block 
(Buus et al., 2008). In both searches, the first block has the theme Patients including this as a 
MeSH-term. The second block has resp. the theme Empowerment and Technology Literacy, and 
these blocks include both MeSH-terms and free text (Buus et al., 2008)(National Center for 
Biotechnology Information, 2021). The MeSH-terms were identified before constructing the block 
searches (National Center for Biotechnology Information, n.d.). In addition, the free text in the 
second block of the first search is truncated (Buus et al., 2008). The block searches are seen on 
resp. figure 15 and figure 16.  
The first search resulted in 12.626 identified articles. First, two rounds of exclusion were 
performed, and after this two rounds of screening. The first was a screening of titles, and the second 
screened by reading the abstract. After this, 4 articles ended up as included. Figure 15 contains the 
flowchart showing this identification. The article by resp. Werbrouck et al. 2018 and Baldoni et 
al. 2017 concern the concept empowerment. In the two other articles, the concept health literacy 
is described (Fernández-Gutiérrez et al., 2018)(S. H. Kim & Lee, 2016). All four articles are placed 
in Appendix 7.  
 
==PAGE:34==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
30 
 
 
Figure 15: The flowchart of first literature search in the fourth series. 
 
==PAGE:35==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
31 
 
From the second search, 1.928 articles were found. Afterwards, one exclusion round and then two 
screening rounds, one of titles and then one of abstracts, were carried out. In the end, 7 articles 
were included in this project. The flowchart visualizing the identification is found on figure 16. 
In five of the articles, concepts are described therefore the articles are placed in Appendix 7. The 
articles by resp. Norman & Skinner 2006 and Shiferaw et al. 2020 concern the concept eHealth 
literacy. In the three other articles, different factors related to the health literacy concept are 
described (Cherid et al., 2020)(Dunn & Hazzard, 2019)(H. Kim & Xie, 2017). The last two articles 
concern design and use of technologies, therefore they are placed in Appendix 6. In the article by 
Baldwin et al. 2017, it’s suggested to combine the design of mobile applications and the 
information from patient portals since this potentially can increase patient empowerment. Finally, 
O’Leary et al. 2015 have identified three patient clusters regarding technology use and care 
involvement, which can support the design of adapted self-management interventions.

more than one of the concepts. Table 5 shows what concept the primary finding of each article 
concerns as well as the specific topic. Based on these articles, it seems like a technology should be 
designed to empower type 2 diabetics by improving their literacies. The last two articles exactly 
describe how to design technologies for self-management and thereby empowerment (Baldwin et 
al., 2017)(O’Leary et al., 2015). All 11 articles are used to discuss the results from the analysis of

3. Design Thinking 
Through the initial searches, knowledge of type 2 diabetics was obtained however this will be 
validated using the Design Thinking process, where the diabetics can be involved. The purpose of 
the process is namely to create user-centric solutions, which can solve problems for the users. It’s 
iterative and consists of five phases. Through these, users and challenges are empathized, problems 
are defined, ideas of solutions are generated, and finally a solution are prototyped and afterwards 
tested (The Interaction Design Foundation, n.d.). Figure 17 gives an overview of the five Design 
==PAGE:39==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
35 
 
Thinking phases and shows the activities within these throughout this project in order to design a 
micro-intervention technology for type 2 diabetics. 
 
Figure 17: The activities in the Design Thinking phases in this project. 
 
The first phase is the Empathize phase, where the purpose is to understand the group of people 
designing a solution for. An understanding of their habits, reasons for these habits, needs, values, 
and perceptions should be obtained. In this way, the designer will gain empathy for the group 
designing for. After the first phase comes the second phase, which is called Define. In this phase, 
the designer will define which challenge to solve for the group based on the findings about them 
obtained through the preceding Empathize phase. This challenge is defined by a point-of-view 
(POV) describing the users, needs, and insights (Hasso Plattner Institute of Design, 2010).  
On the basis of the POV, the third phase Ideate is entered. In this phase, ideas which are solutions 
to the challenge described through the POV are generated. This idea generation can be conducted 
by the use of different techniques. These solutions are turned in to prototypes in the fourth phases, 
which exactly is called Prototype. Prototypes are something possible for a user to interact with 
e.g., sticky notes. In addition, prototyping can be done with basic materials, without knowing the 
outcome, and within a short time (Hasso Plattner Institute of Design, 2010).  
In the fifth and final phase Test, the created prototypes can be tested. The purpose of testing is to 
get feedback on the prototypes and as well to learn more about the users and the challenge defined 
in the POV. When testing a prototype, the scenario for using the prototype should be framed for 
the testers, and they should then experience the prototype themselves. Based on their interaction 
with and comments related to the prototype, it can be improved by creating new iterations (Hasso 
==PAGE:40==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
36 
 
Plattner Institute of Design, 2010). In this project, the Design Thinking framework will be used to 
design a micro-intervention technology for type 2 diabetics. Figure 17 gives an overview of the 
five Design Thinking phases and shows the activities within these throughout this project. 
 
4. Empathize & Define: Focus group  
In the Empathize and Define phase of the Design Thinking process, a focus group with type 2 
diabetics and a following analysis were conducted (The Interaction Design Foundation, n.d.). 
Concerning the Empathize phase, one way to gain empathy for a group of people is to engage with 
them through a long scheduled conversation based on a few questions. This allows them to tell 
their stories (Hasso Plattner Institute of Design, 2010). A focus group can be used for this purpose. 
A focus group is namely a qualitative method, which involves an investigation of a specific topic 
on group level, whereby empirical data is produced. The chosen scientific theoretical perspective 
for the focus group in this project is social constructivist, where the data produced through 
conversations is context-dependent and changeable. Both the content of the conversations and the 
interactions throughout these are afterwards analyzed. From the social constructivist perspective, 
there are some strengths of a focus group. Firstly, it can be used to obtain data regarding the norms 
of a group (Halkier, 2015). This is useful in this project since the grocery shopping norms of type 
2 diabetics as a group are of interest. Secondly, the data is produced via social interactions, which 
are conversations and social dynamics, where different experiences and understandings are 
compared within the group of people who understand each other. They know how to ask each 
other, therefore the data will end up being more complex (Halkier, 2015). This complex data is 
needed in this project because the technology should be useful for all type 2 diabetics. Thirdly, a 
focus group is an easy way to obtain very concentrated data about a topic, and the methods isn’t 
intrusive (Halkier, 2015).  
 
4.1 Planning of Focus group 
In a focus group, a moderator replaces the interviewer from a classic individual interview due to 
the social interactions. The moderator should as an interviewer listen while at the same time create 
an atmosphere for the social interaction with openness and presence. In addition, the moderator 
==PAGE:41==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
37 
 
should facilitate the social interaction, therefore the moderator is a facilitator. The level of the 
involvement of the moderator is decided by selecting a model for the focus group. There exists 
three models for focus groups with different level of structuring and moderator involvement 
(Halkier, 2015). 
The loose model involving very few open questions, which means a low level of structuring and 
moderator involvement, is used for the initial exploration within a field. Questions in a focus group 
are called start-questions since they should result in discussions between participants. For the focus 
group in this project, the loose model isn’t used since the initial exploration took place through the 
observations and the semi structured interviews in the preceding special course. Another model is 
the strict model, which contains many specific start-questions and exercises, therefore the level of 
structuring and moderator involvement is high. This model is useful when there is a large number 
of participants in order to capture their viewpoints but not their social interactions. The strict model 
isn’t chosen, because the discussions of the type 2 diabetics are relevant for understanding the type 
2 diabetics and thereby which technology to design for them. 
A combination of the two described models is the funnel model. This model has open start-
questions in the beginning with only a little moderator involvement and structuring, however it 
ends with specific start-questions and exercises controlled by the moderator. In this way, the model 
covers both individual perceptions, social interactions between the participants, as well as answers 
to specific research questions of interest (Halkier, 2015). Therefore, the selected model for the 
focus group is the funnel model, where the start-questions and exercises are found in the focus 
group guide in Appendix 8.  
The guide contains four start-questions and two exercises concerning three themes. The first theme 
is grocery shopping habits, and the second theme is grocery shopping challenges. These are chosen 
since the literature searches only have resulted in the 2 articles regarding the habits of type 2 
diabetics in Appendix 2. In addition, only the 7 articles regarding challenges in Appendix 3 is 
identified. A focus group can exactly be used if there isn’t a lot of literature regarding a topic 
(Halkier, 2015). Furthermore, the third theme in the focus group is a technology for grocery 
shopping in supermarkets. Even though many mobile applications are identified in the searches in 
this project and the preceding special course, the functionalities of the technology should be 
decided by the users, therefore the type 2 diabetics should discuss these in a focus group. 
==PAGE:42==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
38 
 
Below these themes, one of the start-questions and the two exercises are inspired by either articles 
identified during the literature searches in this project or characteristics from table 3. Table 6 gives 
an overview of this inspiration. Thereby, the focus group guide is developed based on literature 
searches constituting the initial investigation of the problem statement in resp. this project and the 
preceding special course. The Abductive Grounded Theory research approach is therefore used to 
construct the guide, because when using this approach, interview questions are developed based 
on rounds of literature searches related to problem statement (Rahmani & Leifels, 2018). The focus 
group guide seen in Appendix 11 supported my moderator role. 
 
Table 6: The inspiration for the start-questions and exercises.

The two first start-questions are used to illuminate grocery shopping habits, which is the first theme 
in the focus group guide seen in Appendix 8. The first start-question is open and descriptive, 
because it should make the participants describing a typical shopping trip in a supermarket and 
their experiences in this context. However, the second start-question is open and evaluative. The 
participants are asked to evaluate if they want to change anything about their shopping habits e.g., 
the purchased foods and grocery shopping planning (Halkier, 2015). The two inspiration articles 
for this start-question is seen in table 6. The first is Burch et al. 2018 describing that type 2 diabetics 
aren’t eating according to the recommendations, and the second is the article by Savoca & Miller 
2001 stating that time management has an influence on the eating habits of type 2 diabetics. Both 
articles are described in Appendix 2. 
The second theme challenges involves a start-question and an exercise that are found in the focus 
group guide in Appendix 8. The start-question, which is the third in the guide, is open and 
descriptive (Halkier, 2015). With this start-question, the participants are asked to share the 
challenges they have experienced during grocery shopping in supermarkets. In continuation of 
this, the concrete and evaluative exercise is about sorting challenges, from major to minor. It’s 
placed after the start-question, so the participants initially have been given the possibility to discuss 
challenges based on their experiences (Halkier, 2015). For the exercise, challenges are printed on 
pieces of paper, however the participants are also allowed to write extra challenges on blank pieces 
of paper using a pen. These materials are seen on an image in Appendix 11. Exercises and 
supplementary materials have the potential to encourage and focus discussions related to a specific 
topic (Halkier, 2015). Therefore, the sorting of the challenges is performed individually, and 
afterwards they should share and compare with the rest of the group. 
The first challenge included in the exercise is insufficient information about nutritional content 
placed on foods, while the second challenge is lack of knowledge regarding foods. Limited 
knowledge of useful technologies constitutes the third challenge. The fourth challenge is lack of 
support from family or friends, and the fifth challenge is concerning economic factors. These five 
challenges are in articles identified during the first series of literature searches seen in table 6. 
After a short break, the third theme about a technology is discussed on the basis of a start-question 
and an exercise. The start-question being the fourth is a specific and descriptive question since the 
participants are asked to describe which technology and functionalities that can solve the 
==PAGE:44==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
40 
 
previously discussed challenges. Both this start-question and the exercise are found in the focus 
group guide in Appendix 8. In continuation of the start-question, a concrete and evaluative exercise 
is planned. The exercise is placed after the start-question to first have a broad discussion about a 
useful technology in the context of grocery shopping in supermarkets (Halkier, 2015). 
This exercise is the second in the focus group and involves like the first exercise sorting of pieces 
of paper. This time, the pieces contain functionalities for a technology, and the participants are 
again allowed to add their own inputs on blank pieces of paper with a pen. Appendix 11 contains 
an image of these materials. The functionalities found in the focus group guide in Appendix 8 are 
inspired by most of the characteristics in table 3 in section 2.3. Only the four characteristics being 
Shareable shopping lists, Support of shopping lists creation, Scan and or search for foods, and 
Notifications aren’t used as inspiration. The reason is that these are either very specific or basic. 
Table 6 shows the characteristics, which have inspired the functionalities included in the exercise. 
In the exercise, the participants should divide the functionalities into three categories being very 
relevant, medium relevance, and not relevant. As well, they should select the three most important 
functionalities to include in a technology with the purpose to promote healthy grocery shopping 
habits in supermarkets. To encourage discussions, the participants are once again asked to 
complete the exercise first individually and then discuss it within the group. 
Furthermore, the guide contains an introduction in the beginning and as well an ending part. In the 
ending part, the participants are thanked for their participation and introduced to another project, 
which they could be interested in. Both the ending part and the introduction are seen in the focus 
group guide in Appendix 8. The introduction is an important part of a focus group since the 
moderator can take the control in the setting and set the framework for the social interactions. The 
first part of the introduction should be an explanation of the purpose of the study (Halkier, 2015). 
The focus group guide contains a presentation of the project’s purpose as the first in the 
introduction. Another part of the introduction can be a round of introduction (Halkier, 2015). This 
is the second part of the introduction in the focus group guide. 
In addition, it’s important to explain what the focus group is about (Halkier, 2015). The first point 
of the third part in the introduction is exactly describing the three themes of the focus group. The 
focus group format is as well relevant to introduce especially to differentiate between a focus group 
and a classic interview since this is the format that most people know. In addition, the moderator 
==PAGE:45==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
41 
 
involvement as well as the number of start-questions and exercises should also be mentioned 
(Halkier, 2015). The second point of the third part of the introduction explains the format of the 
focus group, the moderator involvement, and as well the number of questions and exercises. 
Furthermore, a comfortable atmosphere is created by explaining the sound recording and pictures 
captured, that the participants should just imagine being in one of their homes, and that there aren’t 
any right or wrong answers. There are no right or wrong answers since the moderator’s purpose is 
to learn something namely the experiences and perceptions of the participants (Halkier, 2015). 
Figure 18 provides an overview of the focus group guide found in Appendix 8.  
 
 
Figure 18: The structure of the focus group guide. 
 
==PAGE:46==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
42 
 
4.2 Recruitment of Participants  
First of all, it was decided how many participants to invite for the focus group. Typically, the 
number of participants appropriate for a focus group is between 5 and 8. However, the number is 
dependent on factors such as purpose, experience, and passion (Krueger & Casey, 2014). The 
purpose of this focus group has been to understand the grocery shopping habits and the challenges 
of type 2 diabetics as well as which technology according to them can support their grocery 
shopping in supermarkets. The diabetics have a lot of experience with these topics since they are 
living with the disease. As well, they may be passionate about these topics and therefore have a 
lot to contribute with since the technology being designed potentially can benefit themselves by 
supporting them during their grocery shopping in supermarkets. Based on this purpose to 
understand, this high level of experience, and this tendency to be passionate, it should be a small 
focus group with between 4 and 6 participants (Krueger & Casey, 2014). Therefore, it was decided 
to invite six participants to the focus group. 
Overall, the selection of these participants was analytically selective, where important 
characteristics for the participants are identified based on the problem statement (Halkier, 2015). 
In this project, the participants should be diagnosed with type 2 diabetics and be grocery shopping 
in supermarkets in order to answer the problem statement in the introduction. Another inclusion 
criteria is that the participants should be between 20 and 60 years old since it’s assumed that most 
people in this age do grocery shopping in supermarkets. These three inclusion criteria, the project’s 
purpose, the outcome for the participants, the focus group, and the co-design workshop were 
included in the recruitment page on the website ForskningNu, which supports research projects in 
the recruitment process of participants (ForskningNu, n.d.). Appendix 9 contains a picture of the 
recruitment page.  
On the 30th of August 2021, the recruitment page about was posted on ForskningNu, and they 
shared the page on social media on the 31st of August 2021 (ForskningNu, n.d.). People, who were 
interested in participating in this project, submitted a form including their personal and contact 
information and a confirmation of meeting the inclusion criteria. The submitted forms were sent 
to my mailbox. The personal and contact information from the inquiries were saved in a secure 
Excel sheet stored in OneDrive.  
==PAGE:47==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
43 
 
In total, 11 inquiries were obtained between the 31st of August 2021 and the 6th of September 2021. 
On the 2nd, 3rd, and 6th of September 2021, the first email was sent to the type 2 diabetics. All the 
sent emails are marked in the Excel sheet, and Appendix 10 contains the text of the first email. 
The email thanked for their inquiries, informed about the purpose of the project, and asked them 
to provide their age and gender. This information was used for selecting the participants for the 
focus group and the co-design workshop. In the Excel sheet, this information is stored, and the 
selection of participants is marked. This selection was performed on the 7th and 8th of September 
2021. Figure 19 gives an overview of the recruitment process for this project. 
 
 
Figure 19: The recruitment process in this project.  
 
When selecting the participants for a focus group, it’s important to find the right combination of 
people, which results in a good type of social interaction. The participants shouldn’t be too 
homogenous since this could result in limited and uninteresting social interaction. Likewise, it 
should neither be too heterogeneous a group, because this can lead to many conflicts or that the 
participants cannot understand each other (Halkier, 2015). In this project, the focus group was 
chosen to involve the most motivated participants and to be as diverse as possible in terms of age 
and gender. They were considered to be motivated if they responded to the first communication 
seen in Appendix 10. In addition, the reason for creating a diverse focus group was that the 
technology should be able to promote healthy grocery shopping habits in supermarkets for as many 
different type 2 diabetics as possible. If this should lead to a very heterogenous group of 
participants, they would have two things in common namely their motivation regarding this project 
and type 2 diabetes. Therefore, they would hopefully be able to listen and understand each other.  
2 of the 11 type 2 diabetics weren’t selected since they didn’t reply to the first email with their age 
and gender. The other three deselections were based on their age and gender meaning that the 
==PAGE:48==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
44 
 
chosen participants resulted in the greatest age diversity within the two genders. The six selected 
participants for both the focus group and the co-design workshop were 3 women and 3 men. The 
women are aged 26, 50, and 59 years, while the men are resp. 54, 56, and 58 years old. The most 
inquiries have been from type 2 diabetics in their fifties, therefore this has been the most diverse 
that the group of participants could get. 
 
4.3 Communication with Participants  
After the selection of participants, the second email was sent on the 8th of September 2021. The 
six selected participants received an email explaining the two activities in this project and 
informing that a date and a location for the focus group will be found. The email for the two type 
2 diabetics, who weren’t selected since they didn’t respond, thanked them for their inquiry and 
informed them that they were deselected. The three type 2 diabetics being deselected due to their 
age were as well thanked in an email and informed about the reason for the deselection. The text 
of all three emails are found in Appendix 10. 
The third communication with the selected participants occurred on the 16th of September 2021, 
and the email contained the date and time for the focus group. As well, it informed them about the 
sound recording of the focus group and their anonymous appearance in this project. Finally, they 
were asked to confirm their participation and to fill out the attached consent form. Appendix 10 
contains the text of the third email. 5 out of the 6 selected participants confirmed their participation 
in the focus group.  
The fourth and last communication prior to the focus group took place on the 21st of September 
2021, and the email text is found in Appendix 10. It informed the participants about the specific 
location at DTU for the focus group. As well, the email did contain a reminder about the date and 
time as well as that they would have to sign the consent form before their participation. Only two 
participants signed the consent form prior to the focus group, therefore printed consent forms seen 
in Appendix 11 were brought on the day of the focus group.  
On the 25th of September 2021, one selected participant decided to opt out. On the day of the focus 
group, the 29th of September 2021, three selected participants cancelled due to sickness. These 
four cancellation were marked in the Excel sheet. Afterwards, two emails were sent to two of the 
==PAGE:49==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
45 
 
previously deselected type 2 diabetics. These emails were sent on resp. the 27th and 29th of 
September. Unfortunately, they didn’t respond to the email. The final number of focus group 
participants was only two. Figure 20 provides an overview of the communication, cancellations, 
and number of participants for the focus group.

4.4 Results 
The focus group took place Wednesday the 29th of September 2021 from approximately 17:00 to 
19:00 PM. The room for the focus group seen from one of the images in Appendix 11. Regarding 
the participants, the first participant (P1) is a 54 year old man working in a municipality as 
technician. He has been living with type 2 diabetes for 15 years, and his diagnose is caused by 
lifestyle. The second participant (P2) is a woman aged 50 years, who is working in the Danish 
Parliament. Approximately a year ago, she was diagnosed with type 2 diabetes, and the cause was 
a weight loss. The background is that both her parents are diagnosed with type 2 diabetes. Today, 
she doesn’t have the diagnosis since she has successfully eaten her way out of the disease, however 
the disease will return if she changes her diet since her blood glucose increases a lot by 
carbohydrate intake. As well, stress affects her blood glucose level, and the same goes for the first 
participant, which blood sugar also increases when he’s irritated.  
==PAGE:50==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
46 
 
During the focus group, the two participants performed the two exercises described in section 4.1. 
After each of the exercises, pictures were taken of the participants’ individual results from each 
exercise. The result of the first exercise is challenges experienced in the context of grocery 
shopping in supermarkets, which are sorted from major to minor. Both participants decided to 
place the major challenge at the top of the list, and the minor challenge at the bottom of the list. 
Figure 21 shows the sorting of all the challenges by both participants.  
Both of them chose to add one extra challenge using a blank piece of paper, and this extra challenge 
was by both placed as the major challenge. The first participant added the challenge “Manglende 
forståelse”, which is the fact people around him especially his co-workers don’t understand and 
accept his decision about eating a healthy diet with a low amount of sugar. The second participant 
decided to add approximately the same challenge namely “Social kontakt”. According to her, this 
involves that it can be challenging to socialize with her family since they either don’t understand 
or support her choice of diet, which is called LCHF (Madbanditten, n.d.).  
As the second biggest challenge, the first participant has placed economic factors since he finds a 
diabetes-friendly diet expensive, whereas the second participant thinks it’s lack of support from 
family and friends, which is closely connected to her major challenge. Placed as the third biggest 
challenge for the first participant is limited knowledge of foods, because it according to him is 
important for type 2 diabetics to possess this knowledge. However, the second participant has 
placed economic factors as the third biggest challenge, because she has accepted that the LCHF 
diet is expensive.  
At the bottom of the list on figure 21, the first participant has added limited knowledge of useful 
technologies, insufficient nutritional information placed on foods, and then as the minor challenge 
lack of support from family and friends. However, he mentioned that the challenge about resp. 
technologies and nutritional information could as well be placed as the third biggest challenge. 
The bottom of the second participant’s list contains insufficient nutritional information placed on 
foods, limited knowledge regarding foods, and lastly limited knowledge of useful technologies as 
the minor challenge. According to her, these aren’t challenges. Especially, she explained that the 
challenge about technologies isn’t relevant for her since she very interested in and uses 
technologies to understand and control her type 2 diabetes. 
 
==PAGE:51==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
47 
 
 
Figure 20: The sorting of challenges by P1 (left) and P2 (right).  
 
The result of the second exercise in the focus group is three categories containing functionalities 
for a technology, which should promote healthy grocery shopping habits in supermarkets. One 
containing the very relevant functionalities, another with the functionalities of medium relevance, 
and a last one involving the not relevant functionalities. As well, the three most important 
functionalities among the very relevant functionalities are identified.  
The first participant has decided only to place the category of high relevance to the left and in this 
to include three of the functionalities. These are nutritional information of foods, personalization, 
and food diary incl. goals. In addition, he mentioned that the personalization should be of shopping 
==PAGE:52==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
48 
 
lists, recipes, and nutritional information as stated on the piece of paper. In the category containing 
the functionalities of medium relevance, the first participant has placed gamification elements, 
recommendations and suggestions, social community, meal plans, shopping lists, and as well 
recipes. Finally, the functionality payment and receipts and the functionality discounts and 
coupons are categorized by the first participant as functionalities not of relevance. Figure 22 shows 
the three categories with functionalities created by the first focus group participant. 
 
 
Figure 22: The categorization of functionalities by P1. 
 
Opposite the first participant, the second participant has decided to place the category containing 
the very relevant functionalities to the right. In the category, she has placed the seven 
functionalities being nutritional information of foods, gamification elements, food diary incl. 
goals, social community, recommendations and suggestions, payment and receipts, as well as 
personalization. The second participant has like the first participant chosen nutritional information 
of foods as one of the three most important functionalities, however she decided to add “scanning” 
to it. With this addition, she meant that a scanner could be used to get information about how foods 
affect her blood sugar. According to her, the two other most important functionalities are 
gamification elements and food diary incl. goals. To the food diary, she added “scanner” since she 
would like to be able to register foods to the diary by scanning with the camera on her phone. Like 
==PAGE:53==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
49 
 
the first participant, the second participant’s medium relevance category contains meal plans, 
shopping lists, and recipes. In the last category with functionalities not of relevance, the second 
participant has only included the functionality discounts and coupons. The three categories of 
functionalities made by the second participant are seen on figure 23. 
 
 
Figure 23: The categorization of functionalities by P2. 
 
4.5 Method for analysis 
In the week after the focus group, it was transcribed based on the sound recording. The 
transcription was therefore performed shortly after and as well completed as fast as possible. This 
was done to make it easier to remember the social interactions during the focus group (Tanggaard 
et al., 2015). Appendix 12 contains the transcribed focus group. After the transcription, the coding 
of the transcribed focus group was performed via the Abductive Grounded Theory process. This 
process was used since it like the framework for this project being Design Thinking process is 
iterative (Rahmani & Leifels, 2018)(The Interaction Design Foundation, n.d.). The first coding 
step in Abductive Grounded Theory process is Open coding, which in itself is an iterative process. 
This process consists of reading the transcribed, creating open codes, and identifying categories. 
Using the computer application NVivo 12, the process resulting in open codes and categories was 
completed (Rahmani & Leifels, 2018).  
==PAGE:54==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
50 
 
The open codes are given a name between one sentence and or several sentences, which state the 
concept described by the specific participant (Rahmani & Leifels, 2018). As well, the name is in 
Danish to be text-oriented and involves either P1 or P2 to indicate the participant, who described 
the concept. If a participant described the same concept more than once, the name of the first open 
code for this concept is reused. However, all social interactions during the focus group weren’t 
coded due to no relevancy in relation to the problem statement. These interactions are questions 
with the purpose to understand a start-question and the concluding dialogue about the upcoming 
co-design workshop, other projects, and related topics. In addition, statements about e.g., the 
Danish population or diabetic products, personal information e.g., age and employment. After the 
creation of open codes, they were categorized into themes based on the concepts being described. 
These categories were then given a label in English (Rahmani & Leifels, 2018). 
Afterwards, the second coding step in the Abductive Grounded Theory process called Axial coding 
was performed. This is likewise an iterative process, where the categories from the Open coding 
were connected to each other if they were related. In order to do this, overall categories were 
created, and the related categories were transformed into subcategories placed below these. After 
this, the third and last coding step being Selective coding was conducted. During this process, the 
categories and the subcategories were validated serval times by comparing them with the findings 
from the literature searches of this project and as well the characteristics in table 3. This process 
is called Theory matching (Rahmani & Leifels, 2018).  
After the Abductive Grounded Theory process, the categories constitute the overall subjects from 
the focus group, whereas the subcategories highlight the different and specific subjects within 
these. Some subjects are related to theory being findings in the preceding special course or this 
project, while others are only found empirically via the focus group. The categories, subcategories, 
and the number of open codes developed during the Abductive Grounded Theory process are seen 
in Appendix 13. Overall, this process is included in the Define phase in the Design Thinking 
framework since the findings from the focus group are synthesized during the Abductive Grounded 
Theory process (Hasso Plattner Institute of Design, 2010). 
 
==PAGE:55==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
51 
 
4.6 Analysis 
The result of the Abductive Grounded Theory process is a total of 15 categories. These categories, 
their subcategories, and the number of open codes below each subcategory are placed in Appendix 
13. Afterwards, all categories except two are separated into six themes concerning type 2 diabetics. 
These two categories are Causes of type 2 diabetes and Factors influencing blood sugar, which in 
section 4.4 are used for the description of the two focus group participants. The rest of the 
categories separated into the six themes are seen in table 7. In this section, all subcategories within 
these themes will be described, and some will be related to articles either found in this project or 
the preceding special course. As well, some subcategories are related to characteristics in table 3 
and concepts in Appendix 7. 
 
Table 7: Overview of the categories below the six themes.

4.6.1 Differences among Type 2 diabetics 
Three of the categories from the Abductive Grounded Theory process underline that type 2 
diabetics are different despite the diagnosis they have in common. These categories are Needs of 
type 2 diabetics, Management of type 2 diabetes, and Useful technological aids for type 2 diabetes. 
Table 8 shows the categories and their subcategories as well as the subcategories’ related articles, 
and their related concepts from Appendix 7. Some of the articles are identified in the literature 
==PAGE:56==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
52 
 
searches in the preceding special course. In addition, the subcategories of the third category in 
table 8 don’t relate to articles and concepts since the subcategories describe the use of specific 
technologies.  
 
Table 8: Subcategories of the three categories regarding differences with related article(s) and concept(s).

Name of subcategory

The first subcategory of the category Needs of type 2 diabetics states that type 2 diabetics are 
different. This is described by both focus group participants, where the first participant stated this 
difference, “(…) vi er så forskellige alle sammen (…), selvom vi alle sammen er diabetikere.” In 
this context, they discussed the different severity of type 2 diabetes, and the second participant 
explained this, “(…) men der er meget stor forskel på, hvor ødelagt ens krop er, eller hvor dårligt 
ens insulin (…) kan fungere som signalstof.”  
The second subcategory as well highlights the difference of type 2 diabetics and concerns that 
some type 2 diabetics need guidance while others don’t. The second participant finds guidance 
==PAGE:57==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
53 
 
unnecessary and provocative since she knows more about type 2 diabetes herself. Opposite, the 
first participant describes guidance as something useful for himself and other type 2 diabetics. This 
need for guidance relates to the article by Stotz et al. 2021, which highlights nutritional counseling 
as a motivation intervention to promote a healthy diet for type 2 diabetics (Stotz et al., 2021). 
Wayman & Madhvanath 2015 describes automated and individual dietary guidance to nudge for 
healthier grocery shopping habits. 
Furthermore, the need for guidance can be related to two concepts, food literacy and health literacy. 
Food literacy is about empowering people with knowledge, which makes them capable of choosing 
healthier foods (Bomfim et al., 2020)(Bomfim & Wallace, 2018). A high food literacy is therefore 
associated with a healthy diet (Vaitkeviciute et al., 2015). Regarding health literacy, it consists of 
the three dimensions being functional, interactive, and critical. The critical dimension involves 
skills to make informed decisions, and guidance can support the development of these skills related 
to dietary choices (Fernández-Gutiérrez et al., 2018). The third and last subcategory Needs of type 
2 diabetics concerns that some type 2 diabetics have the need for motivation. This is a need for the 
second participant, and her motivation is that her nurse is impressed by her low blood sugar. 
The category Management of type 2 diabetes contains five subcategories describing different ways 
type 2 diabetics manage their disease. Diet and individual diet are two of these, which are 
mentioned by the second participant. The first subcategory, Diet, is concerning the fact that she 
has eaten her way out of type 2 diabetes by the LCHF diet. In the third subcategory, Individual 
diet, she described that type 2 diabetics have to find their own diet adjusted to their blood sugar. 
This relates to two articles identified in the preceding special course. These articles describe 
Human-Food Interaction (HFI), which focuses on food habits of people in order to design useful 
and adapted technologies (Comber et al., 2014)(Comber et al., 2012). The second participant uses 
her diet to manage type 2 diabetes, however the first participant takes medication, which is 
described in the fourth subcategory. However, the second subcategory shows that both participants 
uses both exercise and meditation to maintain a stable blood sugar.  
Finally, the second participant uses technology to manage the disease, which constitutes the fifth 
subcategory. She has the continuous glucose monitoring (CGM) system named Veri, which 
measures her blood glucose level. In an app connected to a sensor, she can add images of consumed 
foods and thereby understand how different foods affect her blood glucose (Veri, n.d.). This use 
==PAGE:58==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
54 
 
of technology is related to the article by O’Leary et al. 2015 since it highlights three different 
clusters of chronically diseased patients. The second participant belongs to the cluster called 
“Proactive Techies” (O’Leary et al., 2015). Therefore, she may have a high eHealth literacy, which 
involves to understand, evaluate, and use health information obtained from a technology to target 
a health problem (Norman & Skinner, 2006).  
Furthermore, the technology Veri constitutes the third subcategory of the category Useful 
technological aids for type 2 diabetes (Veri, n.d.). The two other subcategories are technological 
aids used by both of the participants. These are resp. a blog from the association 
Diabetesforeningen concerning recipes and the Diabetes 2 Danmark website with relevant content 
for type 2 diabetics.  
Overall, the three categories Needs of type 2 diabetics, Management of type 2 diabetes, and Useful 
technological aids for type 2 diabetes highlight that type 2 diabetics are different regarding their 
need for guidance and motivation and as well in terms of management. They have different diets, 
use of medication, and use of technology. Therefore, this indicates that there is a need for the 
characteristic Personalization identified in the preceding special course if a technology should 
promote healthy grocery shopping habits in supermarkets for all type 2 diabetics. In addition, the 
technology shouldn’t be too comparable to the already existing CGM, Veri. 
 
4.6.2 Habits of Type 2 diabetics 
In the previous section, it’s highlighted that type 2 diabetics have different diets. This section will 
explore their specific eating habits, grocery shopping habits, and goals regarding these habits. 
These are covered by three categories from the Abductive Grounded Theory process, which are 
Eating habits for type 2 diabetics, Grocery shopping habits, and Goals related to habits. Table 9 
contains the categories and their subcategories and as well shows if the subcategories are related 
to articles found during the literature searches in this project or the preceding special course. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:59==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen

The subcategories of the category Eating habits give an insight into the eating habits of the two 
type 2 diabetics, who participated in the focus group. The first subcategory concerns d eating 
habits. The first participant described that he sometimes chooses the easy and unhealthy, while the 
second participant mentioned that she previously felt sorry for herself and therefore ate unhealthy 
foods. These unhealthy habits can be related to the article by Gardsten et al. 2018, where focus 
group interviews identified choosing healthy foods as a challenge for type 2 diabetics. 
Opposite, the healthy eating habits are described in the second, third, and fourth subcategory. The 
second subcategory involves a description of the first focus group participant’s healthy diet, which 
he has been following the last couple of years. Therefore, his diet may be more aligned with the 
dietary recommendations compared to the diet of other type 2 diabetics (Burch et al., 2018).  
==PAGE:60==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
56 
 
The diet of the second focus group participant is described in the third subcategory. Her diet is 
called LCHF, which helps to manage the blood sugar level by involving a lot of fat e.g., from 
cream and very few carbohydrates. According to her, the LCHF diet is easier than the KETO diet, 
which both participants have tried, due to a higher amount of carbohydrates accepted. The low 
amount of carbohydrates in the second participant’s diet is as well described in the fourth 
subcategory. Overall, the second participant is probably eating less grains but more dairy compared 
to dietary guidelines (Burch et al., 2018). As well, she has been on the LCHF diet in many years 
and knows how many carbohydrates she can tolerate. Therefore, she has a lot of knowledge of 
type 2 diabetes management. Savoca & Miller 2001 mention this knowledge as a factor influencing 
eating habits of type 2 diabetics. 
The last two subcategories of the category Eating habits in table 9 cannot be related to articles 
found through literature searches in this project or the preceding special course. The fifth 
subcategory is about quality foods, which the second participant is passionate about. Therefore, 
she prioritizes to spend money on foods of quality. Both participants are spending money on sugar-
free foods, and this constitutes the sixth subcategory of the category Eating habits.  
The second category in table 9 is Grocery shopping habits, and its three subcategories describe 
how the two focus group participants do their grocery shopping in supermarkets. This can all be 
related to the article by Savoca & Miller 2001 since the two participants have very different grocery 
shopping habits in terms of both social support and time management. These two factors have an 
influence on the eating habits of type 2 diabetics (Savoca & Miller, 2001). The first and third 
subcategory concern the second participant’s grocery shopping habits involving a high level of 
social support and time management.  
The first subcategory explains that her family for many years has done their grocery shopping 
based on weekly meal plans on the basis of LCHF recipes. This indicates a high level of time 
management, which as well is described in the third subcategory. It concerns the weekly grocery 
shopping in supermarkets that the second participant does in collaboration with her husband in a 
very structured way, where they are responsible for the shopping in different departments. 
Therefore, this grocery shopping as well involves a high level of social support. Overall, this 
grocery shopping is effective and on the basis of both routines and health, which is described in 
the article by Kalnikaité et al. 2013 identified in the preceding special course. 
==PAGE:61==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
57 
 
The grocery shopping habits of the first participant constitute the second subcategory below 
Grocery shopping habits in table 9. These habits involve a low level of social support and time 
management since the first participant does grocery shopping in supermarkets almost daily, by 
himself, and based on a loose plan (Savoca & Miller, 2001). According to him, this way of grocery 
shopping sometimes results in spontaneous purchases of unhealthy foods. 
Lastly, table 9 contains the four subcategories of the category Goals related to habits. The fourth 
subcategory indicates that the second participant is satisfied with her eating and grocery shopping 
habits, therefore she didn’t mention any goals. However, the first, second, and third subcategory 
are goals of the first participant. His overall goal constituting the first subcategory is to become 
free of diabetes medication. The third subcategory describes that another goal for him is to plan 
grocery shopping in supermarkets since it would make sure that he doesn’t spontaneously purchase 
unhealthy foods. He would like this plan to be based on recipes in a meal plan, which constitutes 
the second subcategory. This meal plan can promote healthy grocery shopping and thereby be a 
step towards his goal of becoming medication-free. Goal setting and action planning are exactly 
described by Werbrouck et al. 2018 as important elements in patient empowerment interventions. 
 
4.6.3 Challenges for Type 2 diabetics

experience various challenges. These will be described in this section based on two categories 
from the Abductive Grounded Theory process, which are Grocery shopping challenges and 
Challenges of type 2 diabetics. Table 10 contains the categories, their subcategories as well as the 
related articles, characteristics, and concepts of the subcategories. Most of the subcategories are 
related to one or more articles, which are identified either during the literature searches of this 
project or the preceding special course. As well, some subcategories are related to some of the 
characteristics placed in table 3 and the concepts in Appendix 7.
