The first category in table 10 is Grocery shopping challenges, and the subcategories of this 
constitute the different challenges experienced by the two focus group participants in the context 
of grocery shopping in supermarkets. The first subcategory is a challenge mentioned by the first 
participant, and it concerns that he finds it difficult to replace old unhealthy grocery shopping 
habits with new healthy habits. This challenge regarding making the healthy choices are identified 
in other focus groups with type 2 diabetics (Gardsten et al., 2018). Warning labels describing health 
consequences are an effective nudging intervention to reduce unhealthy purchases (Gupta et al., 
2020). Likewise, these purchases can be reduced with traffic light labels and increased prices (von 
Philipsborn et al., 2020). These nudging interventions can empower type 2 diabetics by increasing 
their knowledge of groceries (Bomfim et al., 2020). 
The second subcategory and challenge is Expensive diabetes-friendly foods and medicine, which 
is described by both focus group participants. According to the first participant, it’s expensive have 
type 2 diabetes due to the medicine and the special diabetes-friendly foods. The second participant 
doesn’t experience the expensive foods as a challenge since she has been following the LCHF diet 
for many years. However, she admitted that the foods are expensive, which is an unfair challenge 
that many type 2 diabetics face. Economic factors are identified by both Breuing et al. 2020 and 
==PAGE:64==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
60 
 
Stotz et al. 2021 as a challenge related to type 2 diabetics. Food-pricing interventions such as 
coupons and discounts can potentially solve this challenge (Gittelsohn et al., 2017)(L. V. Moore 
et al., 2016). Therefore, a solution to the challenge could be to include the characteristic Discounts 
from grocery stores in the technology targeted type 2 diabetics. These discounts could lead to an 
increased purchase of healthy groceries and a decrease regarding unhealthy foods (Gittelsohn et 
al., 2017). 
In table 10, the third subcategory concerns insufficient nutrition labels as a challenge. According 
to the first participant, these don’t include enough details about nutrients e.g., unsaturated and 
saturated fat. It’s problematic that details are missing since many customers use the nutrition labels 
as the guide when grocery shopping (Campos et al., 2011). Kalnikaité et al. 2013 describe that 
technologies have the potential to highlight important information. In section 4.6.1, it’s as well 
described that type 2 diabetics are different, therefore it should be possible for them to personalize 
the nutritional information. This makes the characteristics Access to nutritional information and 
Personalization relevant to include in a technology since they would be able to solve the challenge 
concerning insufficient nutrition labels. In addition, these characteristics could improve the food 
literacy of type 2 diabetics and thereby empower them to perform healthier grocery shopping 
(Bomfim & Wallace, 2018)(Bomfim et al., 2020). 
Likewise, the fourth subcategory in table 10 involves nutritional information. This challenge is 
about lack of knowledge of carbohydrates and is mentioned by both focus group participants. The 
first participant explained that it’s important for type 2 diabetics to know how many carbohydrates 
their groceries can contain, whereas the second participant highlighted that many type 2 diabetics 
don’t know their tolerated amounts. Breuing et al. 2020 have identified limited knowledge as a 
common challenge related to type 2 diabetics. In the context of grocery shopping, some customers 
struggle to understand nutrition labels, therefore it should be ensured that the nutrition labels are 
easy to understand (Campos et al., 2011). A solution is educational interventions such as web-
based learning about nutrition labels, which can lead to increased knowledge (S. G. Moore et al., 
2018).  
Another solution could be a technology providing feedback on the groceries and thereby increasing 
the knowledge of how the groceries affect them (Kalnikaitė et al., 2013). This knowledge could 
be incorporated as the characteristic Support nutritional food choices e.g., suggestions and the 
==PAGE:65==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
61 
 
characteristic Access nutritional information in a technology promoting healthy grocery shopping 
in supermarkets. As well, the knowledge can empower type 2 diabetics’ dietary choices resulting 
in an increased food literacy, which is connected to a healthy dietary intake (Bomfim et al., 
2020)(Vaitkeviciute et al., 2015). 
In table 10, the last two subcategories of the category Grocery shopping challenges cover 
challenges related to the supermarkets’ supply of groceries targeted type 2 diabetics. These 
challenges were described by both focus group participants. The fifth challenge is concerning the 
overall limited selection of diabetes-friendly foods e.g., ready-prepared meals, special products, 
and sugar-free groceries. In addition, these sugar-free groceries are of low quality, which 
constitutes the sixth challenge. The first participant explained that he has been sick due to these 
groceries and that the taste of them is artificial. Overall, this poor supermarket supply of diabetes-
friendly foods can be related to the article by Escaron et al. 2013 identified in the preceding special 
course. This describes supermarket intervention categories that can promote purchases of healthy 
foods, and increased availability of healthy foods is included in two of the categories (Escaron et 
al., 2013). Therefore, the supermarkets’ supply of diabetes-friendly groceries seems to be 
important for supporting healthy grocery shopping habits of type 2 diabetics. 
The second category in table 10 is Challenges of type 2 diabetics, where the 10 subcategories are 
various challenges experienced by type 2 diabetics in their personal life, their social life, and the 
society. The first challenge is personal and about finding the balance with type 2 diabetes, which 
was mentioned by both participants in the focus group. The first participant described finding the 
balance in terms of blood sugar level and diet, while the second participant highlighted the 
challenge of finding the right level of diet and exercise. In addition, the second participant 
mentioned that each type 2 diabetic has to find their individual balance. 
The second challenge below the category Challenges of type 2 diabetics is in the society and 
concerns that the association Diabetesforeningen as well as doctors don’t provide the appropriate 
information about a diabetes-friendly diet. Both focus group participants mentioned this, and as 
well they described doctors’ lack of knowledge of a diabetes-friendly diet. According to the second 
participant, Diabetesforeningen also lack this knowledge. As well, she stated that both 
Diabetesforeningen and doctors recommend an amount of carbohydrates that type 2 diabetics 
cannot tolerate. Insufficient resources about food and nutrition are exactly identified as a challenge 
==PAGE:66==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
62 
 
for type 2 diabetes management (Stotz et al., 2021). In order to solve this challenge, the 
characteristic Support nutritional food choices being useful recommendations, tips, and 
suggestions related to diabetes-friendly foods could be included in a technology for type 2 
diabetics. Likewise, the characteristic Access nutritional information, which is about giving 
appropriate nutritional information, could be incorporated. These two characteristics would 
improve the food literacy among type 2 diabetics and thereby empowerment related to dietary 
choices, which can lead to a healthy diet (Bomfim et al., 2020)(Vaitkeviciute et al., 2015). 
In addition to the insufficient dietary information from Diabetesforeningen, the association doesn’t 
have offers suiting all type 2 diabetics. This constitutes the third challenge related to the second 
category in table 10 and was mentioned by both focus group participants. Diabetesforeningen 
places their group offers in the morning, where some type 2 diabetics are at work. The first 
participant stated that the association is assuming that type 2 diabetics are pensioners. Therefore, 
the group offers can be seen as insufficient motivating interventions since Diabetesforeningen isn’t 
considering the socioeconomic status of the type 2 diabetics (Stotz et al., 2021).  
A solution to this and as well the second challenge could be to include the characteristic Social 
networking in a technology by creating an online forum with the opportunity to interact with other 
type 2 diabetics and get answers from Diabetesforeningen. This would constitute both a support 
group and nutritional counseling available for type 2 diabetics independent of their socioeconomic 
status (Stotz et al., 2021). By this, their food literacy could be improved and thereby they could be 
empowered to choose healthy foods (Bomfim et al., 2020)(Vaitkeviciute et al., 2015). As well, the 
fourth challenge concerns the limitations of Diabetesforeningen’s content and could be solved with 
an online forum. The challenge, which is the limited suggestions of training exercises on different 
levels, is experienced by the second participant. As well, training groups at different levels aren’t 
included in her local association.  
The fifth and the ninth challenge below the category Challenges of type 2 diabetics in table 10 are 
closely connected and are both appearing in the society. Overall, they concern the way type 2 
diabetics are treated by doctors. The fifth challenge is the doctors’ lack of knowledge, where the 
first participant explained that doctors don’t know enough about type 2 diabetes. In addition, the 
information from the doctors is useless according to both focus group participants and constitutes 
the ninth challenge. The second participant described an orange booklet that is given to type 2 
==PAGE:67==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
63 
 
diabetics, when they are diagnosed. This booklet contains recipes with an amount of carbohydrates 
that is too high for type 2 diabetics. The first participant added that he didn’t know what to do after 
he got diagnosed. Both healthcare provider issues and education are identified as central challenges 
related to type 2 diabetes management (Wilkinson et al., 2014). These could result in low health 
literacy, which then could be a challenge for the management. Therefore, it’s important that 
healthcare providers improve the health literacy of type 2 diabetics by empowering them (S. H. 
Kim & Lee, 2016).  
Like the first challenge below Challenges of type 2 diabetics, the sixth challenge is one in the 
personal life and concerns the knowledge of how the blood sugar is affected by different factors 
such as foods. The first focus group participant described this and mentioned that it especially is a 
challenge for newly diagnosed. One of the most common challenges for type 2 diabetics is exactly 
lack of knowledge (Breuing et al., 2020). One way to obtain this knowledge is via educational 
interventions about nutrition labels with the purpose to learn how to use these to select appropriate 
foods for the blood sugar (S. G. Moore et al., 2018). Another way is via a technology providing 
feedback being the effect on blood sugar of the purchased groceries (Kalnikaitė et al., 2013). 
Coaching is one of the functionalities in the characteristic Support nutritional food choices, which 
potentially could improve the food literacy of type 2 diabetics by empowering them to choose 
healthy groceries when shopping in supermarkets (Bomfim et al., 2020).  
The seventh challenge is the only challenge below the category Challenges of type 2 diabetics in 
table 10, which is related to the social life. Both participants added this challenge during the first 
exercise in the focus group. It concerns the fact that their social network lack understanding of 
their dietary choices due to type 2 diabetes. The first participant described that both his family and 
co-workers don’t understand his de-selection of unhealthy foods and that the support therefore is 
lacking. The same lack of support is experienced by the second participant especially from her 
family, which struggles to understand the de-selections as a part of her LCHF diet. Breuing et al. 
2020 have identified family and friends as a common challenge related to type 2 diabetes, and 
support is found as a central challenge by Wilkinson et al. 2014. The challenge with lack of 
understanding could be solved by social network interventions about type 2 diabetes management, 
where the social network is educated and engaged since they can lead to improved support 
(Spencer-Bonilla et al., 2017). 
==PAGE:68==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
64 
 
In table 10, the eighth challenge of the category Challenges of type 2 diabetics is a part of the 
personal life and described by both focus group participants. This challenge is a collection of the 
participants’ comments related to the challenges included in the first exercise of the focus group. 
The first participant highlighted that all the presented challenges are experienced by newly 
diagnosed type 2 diabetics, while the second participant commented that the presented challenges 
aren’t experienced by her. Especially, she doesn’t experience the challenge regarding knowledge 
of useful technologies. As mentioned in section 4.6.1, she’s a part of the cluster “Proactive 
Techies”, and therefore her eHealth literacy may be high (O’Leary et al., 2015)(Norman & 
Skinner, 2006). However, the newly diagnosed type 2 diabetics could be in one of the other clusters 
with less engagement related to technology interventions relevant for the type 2 diabetes 
management (O’Leary et al., 2015). A main identified challenge related to the use of these 
interventions is technology literacy (Alvarado et al., 2017).  
The last and tenth challenge in the category Challenges of type 2 diabetics in table 10 is as well 
appearing in the personal life of type 2 diabetics. The challenge is weight loss, which is difficult 
for type 2 diabetics. Both focus group participants mentioned that it’s harder for type 2 diabetics 
to lose weight compared to people without the diagnosis. The first participant added that it’s 
individual which diet that can cause weight loss, while the second participant stated that it’s easy 
for type 2 diabetics to gain weight. Nevertheless, type 2 diabetics are informed by the doctors that 
they should lose weight.  
 
4.6.4 Society solutions targeted Type 2 diabetics 
Some of the challenges for type 2 diabetics described in the previous section can be solved by 
changes in the society, and some of these are already described. In this section, other society 
solutions will be described on the basis of the category Desired changes in society from the 
Abductive Grounded Theory process. This category contains six subcategories constituting the 
solutions. Table 11 shows the category, its subcategories, and the challenges from the previous 
section that can be solved. Only one of the subcategories can potentially solve a challenge, which 
isn’t covered in the previous section. 
 
==PAGE:69==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
65 
 
Table 11: Subcategories of the Desired changes in society category with related challenge. 
No. of 
subcategory

Challenge

The first and fourth subcategory in table 11 are solutions to the challenges regarding the poor 
supermarkets’ supply of diabetes-friendly groceries being the fifth and sixth challenge below the 
category Grocery shopping challenges in table 10. The fifth challenge can be solved by the first 
solution in table 11, which is suggested by both focus group participants. The first participant 
suggested that supermarkets create a diabetes-friendly department with a wider selection of this 
type of foods. The suggestion of the second participant is that supermarkets should have more 
special products, which are appropriate for her LCHF diet. Furthermore, the fourth solution in 
table 11 can solve the sixth challenge. This solution is described by the second focus group 
participant and is that the supermarkets should supply sugar-free groceries of quality with a low 
amount of carbohydrates and a good taste.  
The second challenge of the Grocery shopping challenges in table 10 covers that diabetes-friendly 
foods and medicine are expensive. The high prices on foods can be solved by the second 
subcategory in table 11, which is a suggestion by the first participant. He suggested that politicians 
should make it possible for all type 2 diabetics to get discounts on diabetes-friendly foods. As well, 
his suggestion includes that diabetes should have free access to fitness centers. The third 
subcategory in table 11 is as well something that the politicians have to change in the society. The 
solution is suggested by the second focus group participant and is that all newly diagnosed type 2 
diabetics should be given a free CGM system. This could solve the sixth challenge below the 
==PAGE:70==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
66 
 
category Challenges of type 2 diabetics from table 10 since the CGM system can provide type 2 
diabetics with knowledge of how different factors influence their blood sugar. 
The fifth subcategory in table 11 is described by the second focus group participant. This society 
change covers that restaurants should have courses on their menu with a low amount of 
carbohydrates like they have other special courses e.g., for vegans. However, this change isn’t a 
solution to any of the challenges described in the previous section. Finally, the sixth society change 
in table 11 is an extreme solution to the first challenge below the category Grocery shopping 
challenges in table 10. The solution is to illegalize the sale of unhealthy foods that is suggested by 
both focus group participants. According to the first participant, a great amount of sugar and fat in 
groceries should be outlawed. The second participant’s suggestion is even more extreme being to 
completely illegalize sugar and unhealthy fat in groceries, because it according to her is dangerous.  
 
4.6.5 Empowerment of Type 2 diabetics 
In the section 4.6.3, it’s described how some of the identified challenges of type 2 diabetics can be 
solved by empowerment. This section will explore the concept empowerment and concrete 
suggestions of interventions to empower type 2 diabetics. The category from the Abductive 
Grounded Theory process covering these interventions is called Empowerment, which has four 
subcategories. Table 12 contains the category and the subcategories, and the subcategories’ related 
articles identified in the literature searches of this project. As well, it involves related concepts 
from Appendix 7. 
 
 
==PAGE:71==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
67 
 
Table 12: Subcategories of the Empowerment category with related article and concepts. 
No. of 
subcategory 
Name of subcategory 
Article 
Concepts

1

•

•

•

•

•

•

Overall, the first three subcategories are patient empowerment interventions focusing on 
knowledge, which combined with setting goals and planning actions can empower type 2 diabetics 
(Werbrouck et al., 2018). Such interventions can result in an improvement of clinical parameters 
and increased knowledge concerning type 2 diabetes (Baldoni et al., 2017). The first and second 
intervention are empowering by improving the food literacy, which can result in a heathier diet 
(Bomfim et al., 2020)(Bomfim & Wallace, 2018). As well, the two interventions are improving 
the health literacy especially the critical dimension since the type 2 diabetics get new information 
and based on this they can make empowered decisions (Fernández-Gutiérrez et al., 2018).  
The first subcategory and intervention is in table 12 a guide. According to the first focus group 
participant, this guide should support newly diagnosed type 2 diabetics and those who want 
guidance. The guide should explain how different foods are affecting the blood sugar. This could 
for example be done as nutritional counseling or during cooking classes, which would motivate 
type 2 diabetics to get healthier eating habits (Stotz et al., 2021).  
In table 12, the second subcategory and intervention is a new introduction given by doctors to type 
2 diabetics. First of all, the second participant thinks that type 2 diabetes should be introduced as 
carbohydrate intolerance and that an introduction to the website Diabetes 2 Danmark described in 
section 4.6.1, should be given. According to both focus group participants, the type 2 diabetics 
==PAGE:72==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
68 
 
should as well be informed about the tolerated amount of carbohydrates. The second participant 
added that type 2 diabetics should learn about carbohydrates and how to find them on the nutrition 
labels. Both the basics about carbohydrates, the tolerated amount of carbohydrates, and nutrition 
labels could be introduced via taught sessions either physical or online (S. G. Moore et al., 2018).  
The third subcategory in table 12 covers a patient empowerment intervention based on a 
technology. More specifically, it concerns a glucose monitor to obtain knowledge of how different 
factors affect the blood glucose. The first focus group participant mentioned that he measures his 
blood sugar when starting a new diet, while the factors foods, exercise, and stress were mentioned 
by the second participant. By using a glucose monitor, the second participant gains knowledge of 
her blood glucose and based on this the choices are easier. To benefit from this technology based 
intervention, it requires that type 2 diabetics possess eHealth literacy. This covers that they are 
able to understand the health information from the glucose monitor being the blood sugar level, 
and that they can use this level to make the right decisions regarding the factors influencing their 
type 2 diabetes (Norman & Skinner, 2006). 
While the other subcategories are concrete patient empowerment interventions, the fourth 
subcategory in table 12 is directly concerning the concept patient empowerment, which is about 
focusing on and giving the autonomy to the patient (Werbrouck et al., 2018). Both focus group 
participants described that type 2 diabetics are responsible for managing their own disease. For the 
first participant, it took several years to figure out how to manage the disease himself, ”(…) men 
det har taget mig ufattelig mange år det her for at finde ud af, hvordan man skal behandle sig selv 
egentlig.” This self-management requires a high level of health literacy, which can be improved 
by the first and second intervention described in this section (Fernández-Gutiérrez et al., 2018). 
Likewise, the third intervention involving a healthy technology being a glucose monitor can 
improve the health literacy (Dunn & Hazzard, 2019). In addition, the first participant mentioned 
that type 2 diabetics are in charge of their food intake. Therefore, a high level of food literacy is 
needed, which the first and second intervention potentially can ensure (Bomfim et al., 2020).  
 
4.6.6 Technology solution for Type 2 diabetics 
This section concerns a technology solution, which can promote healthy grocery shopping habits 
by empowering type 2 diabetics. This technology is described by three categories from the 
==PAGE:73==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
69 
 
Abductive Grounded Theory process, which are Technology promoting healthy grocery shopping, 
Functionalities maybe to include in a technology, and Functionalities to include in a technology. 
Table 13 contains these categories, their subcategories, as well as the subcategories’ related articles 
and characteristics. Most of the subcategories of the first category are related to one or more 
articles, which are found in the literature searches of this project or the preceding special course. 
Regarding the last two categories, some of the subcategories are related to articles, however all of 
them are related to one or more characteristics from table 3.

The first category in table 13 is Technology promoting healthy grocery shopping, which overall 
concerns a technology solution that can support type 2 diabetics’ grocery shopping in 
supermarkets. The first subcategory is described by both focus group participants, who highlighted 
that the technology shouldn’t contain too much information, especially not for newly diagnosed 
type 2 diabetics. This relates to the article by Jain et al. 2020, which describe that technologies 
being easy to use are preferred by type 2 diabetics. Having an appropriate amount of information 
could be a factor making the technology easy to use for type 2 diabetics. Likewise, the second 
subcategory is about the content of the technology. According to the first participant, it should 
contain different functionalities so type 2 diabetics only have to use one technology.  
The third subcategory is describing the type of technology, which can promote healthy grocery 
shopping in supermarkets for type 2 diabetics. Both focus group participants mentioned that the 
technology should be a mobile application. For the first participant, this is the appropriate solution 
since it can contain many functionalities. The second participant added that she would like to have 
a glucose sensor, which is placed on the skin and connected to a CGM incorporated in a mobile 
application. A mobile application is an intuitive technology since it can contain user-friendly 
functionalities such as tracking (Baldwin et al., 2017). Based on these, it can provide nutritional 
education and information, which potentially can improve the eating and grocery shopping habits 
(Paramastri et al., 2020). Overall, a mobile application is a eHealth service appropriate for people 
with all levels of health literacy (H. Kim & Xie, 2017).  
The second and third category in table 13 describe the functionalities of a mobile application. The 
subcategories of the second category constitute the functionalities, which maybe could be 
incorporated in a mobile application to promote healthy grocery shopping habits. During the 
second exercise in the focus group, the subcategories’ underlying functionalities are either placed 
==PAGE:75==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
71 
 
in the category of high relevance by only one of the participants, placed the category of medium 
relevance by both participants, or suggested by only one of the participants.  
The first functionality maybe to include in a mobile application is Gamification. The gamification 
elements being games, quizzes, and challenges are placed in the category of medium relevance by 
the first participant, while the second participant has categorized them as high relevance. The 
second participant finds gamification elements fun and motivating. As well, she compares the 
elements with the CGM system Veri since it for her becomes a game to keep the blood sugar low 
(Veri, n.d.). The subcategory gamification is naturally related to the characteristic Gamification, 
which 10 of the identified mobile applications contain as seen in table 3.  
The second functionality in table 13 maybe to include is Lists of foods, where one contains 
diabetes-friendly foods and the other involves foods that aren’t diabetes-friendly. These are 
suggested by the first participant since he struggles to remember the diabetes-friendliness of foods 
and thinks that other type 2 diabetics struggle too. Opposite, the second participant don’t want 
these lists especially not the list containing the non-diabetes-friendly foods, because this list would 
be very long due to her strict LCHF diet. This shows that type 2 diabetics are different as concluded 
in section 4.6.1. In addition, the lists of foods can be compared with the scoring system described 
in the article by Nikolova & Inman 2015 identified in the preceding special course. The system 
made it easier for customers to choose healthy groceries (Nikolova & Inman, 2015). Furthermore, 
the lists of foods are related to the characteristic Support nutritional food choices since the lists are 
a form of recommendation. Table 3 shows that the characteristic is in 23 of the identified mobile 
applications. 
Likewise, the fourth functionality maybe to include in a mobile application is related to the 
characteristic Support nutritional food choices since both recommendations and suggestions are 
included in the characteristic. In the second exercise in focus group, the first participant has placed 
the functionality in the category of medium relevance, while the second participant has categorized 
it as very relevant. However, the second participant afterwards indicated that it isn’t one of the 
most important functionalities. The recommendations can be related to the to three articles found 
during the literature searches of this project, which each are introducing a recommender system. 
The system of Wayman & Madhvanath 2015 provides recommendations based on individual goals 
and previous purchases. Likewise, the system by Li et al. 2009 uses purchases as a factor, and it 
==PAGE:76==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
72 
 
recommends similar groceries. Finally, the third recommender system is based on personal 
preferences and price sensitivity (Wan et al., 2017). 
In table 13, the third functionality in the category of functionalities maybe to include is Purchase. 
The reason for this placement is that the first focus group participant has categorized the 
functionality payment and receipts as not of relevance, whereas the second participant has placed 
it in the category of high relevance. According to her, purchase is appropriate to have in a mobile 
application, but it isn’t the most important functionality. The naturally related characteristic is 
Purchase, which only is involved in 7 of the identified mobile applications as seen in table 3. 
The fifth functionality maybe to include in a mobile application is actually two functionalities 
combined namely shopping lists and recipes. These two functionalities are by both focus group 
participants placed in the category of medium relevance during the second exercise. The first 
participant added that both diabetes-friendly recipes and shopping lists are useful. The shopping 
lists are naturally related to the characteristic Shopping lists. Table 3 shows that it’s the 
characteristic, which most of the identified mobile applications contain being 46. Furthermore, the 
recipes are related to the characteristic Collection of recipes. This is present in 33 of the identified 
mobile applications making it the fourth most common characteristic in table 3. 
The sixth functionality in the category of those maybe to include in a mobile application is Social 
networking, which in the second exercise in the focus group is represented as social community. 
The functionality is placed in the category of medium relevance by the first participant., while the 
second participant has categorized it as very relevant. However, afterwards she added that many 
mobile applications have a social community, which don’t function as intended. The functionality 
is related to the characteristic Social networking. As seen in table 3, this characteristic is included 
in 11 of the identified mobile applications. 
The third category in table 13 consists of the functionalities, which according to the two focus 
group participants should be included in a mobile application since they can promote healthy 
grocery shopping. In the second focus group exercise, the subcategories’ underlying functionalities 
are either categorized very relevant by both participants or a suggestion by one participant, which 
was supported by the other participant. The first subcategory Access to nutritional information is 
included as the functionality nutritional information of foods in the second exercise. Both 
participants have placed it as one of the three most important in the category of high relevance, 
==PAGE:77==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
73 
 
and the second participant added that she especially is interested in the amount of carbohydrates. 
The functionality naturally relates to the characteristic Access to nutritional information, which is 
present in 20 of the identified mobile applications as seen in table 3.  
The second functionality of functionalities to include in a mobile application is Food diary incl. 
goal setting. In the second exercise in the focus group, the two participants have categorized this 
functionality food diary incl. goals as very relevant. The second participant explained that the food 
dairy should be as simple as possible by making the users able to add foods by taking a picture. 
Based on the picture, the food diary should be able to calculate the amount of calories since it 
according to the second participant is important for the type 2 diabetes management. The food 
diary incl. goal setting functionality is related to the characteristics Food diary and Goal setting. 
Table 3 shows that these are included in resp. 31 and 14 of the identified mobile applications, 
which makes Food diary the fifth most present.  
During the second focus group exercise, the second participant suggested that type 2 diabetics 
should be able to use the mobile application to get to know how much different groceries affect 
their blood sugar. Over time, the mobile application must obtain these effects. In addition, the 
effects should be obtained by scanning the groceries while shopping in supermarkets. This 
suggestion was supported by the first participant, however he added that a connected glucose 
monitor is needed. In table 13, this functionality suggested by the second participant constitutes 
the fifth subcategory below the functionalities to include in a mobile application. The functionality 
relates to two of the characteristics. The first is Support nutritional food choices, because it covers 
coaching, which the fifth functionality can be seen as. The second related characteristic is Scan 
and or search for foods since the effect on the blood sugar should be obtained by scanning 
groceries. As seen in table 3, 23 of the identified mobile applications contain Support nutritional 
food choices, while Scan and or search for foods is the third most present characteristic being 
included in 35. 
The fifth functionality is connected to both the third and fourth subcategory. The fourth 
subcategory covers the functionality individual adjustment, which is included in the second focus 
group exercise. Both participants have placed the functionality in the category of high relevance. 
This functionality relates to the characteristic Personalization, and table 3 shows that 23 mobile 
applications with this characteristic are found. In addition, the second participant mentioned that 
==PAGE:78==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
74 
 
personalization is a part of her suggestion about scanning groceries to obtain the individual effect 
on blood sugar, which constitutes the fifth functionality.  
Likewise, the third subcategory being the functionality Goal setting is connected to the fifth 
subcategory. The reason is that the second participant during the second exercise suggested that it 
should be possible to add individual goals for the blood sugar level and to be updated when the 
goals are reached. This suggestion was supported by the first participant. This suggested Goal 
setting functionality is naturally related to the characteristic Goal setting. As seen in table 3, the 
characteristic is involved in 14 of the identified mobile applications. Both the third, fourth, and 
fifth subcategory are related to the article by Flaherty 2019 from the preceding special course. In 
this article, it’s exactly described that mobile applications including self-monitoring and goal 
setting can influence grocery shopping habits if they are adjusted to the induvial (Flaherty, 2019). 
In table 13, the sixth functionality in the category of those to include in a mobile application is 
Understanding of quantity. This is connected to the first functionality in this category about 
nutritional information since this information according to the second participant doesn’t matter if 
type 2 diabetics eat large portions. Therefore, she suggested to include an indication of appropriate 
portion sizes, and this suggestion was supported by the first participant. This suggested 
functionality relates to the characteristic Support nutritional food choices since it involves e.g., 
recommendations and tips, which could be used for supporting type 2 diabetics in consuming 
appropriate portion sizes. Table 3 shows that 23 of the identified mobile applications contain this 
characteristic.  
 
4.7 Conclusion on Focus group 
After this detailed description of all the subcategories in the six themes, one challenge for type 2 
diabetics in the context of grocery shopping in supermarkets will be selected. The challenge will 
be defined by constructing a point-of-view (POV) involving the users, one need or a limited 
number of needs, and the insights from synthesizing via the Abductive Grounded Theory process 
(Hasso Plattner Institute of Design, 2010). Therefore, the POV is a step towards answering the 
problem statement in the introduction. 
==PAGE:79==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
75 
 
In order to decide on a POV, deselections have to be made. First of all, the theme Habits of Type 
2 diabetics describing habits related to eating and grocery shopping as well as goals for the habits 
aren’t included in the POV since they constitute what could be affected by the technology. The 
same applies for the personal challenges below the category Challenges of type 2 diabetics being 
the first, eighth, and tenth. In addition, the challenges in the same category appearing in the society 
more specifically with associations and doctors, which are the second, third, fourth, fifth, and ninth 
subcategory, aren’t included. As well, the last two subcategories of the category Grocery shopping 
challenges covering challenges regarding the supermarket supply of diabetes-friendly groceries 
are deselected. The overall reason for deselecting all these challenges is that they aren’t possible 
to solve with a technology and within the scope of this project. The desired changes in the society 
below the theme Society solutions targeted Type 2 diabetics are as well outside the scope.  
Furthermore, the two first patient empowerment interventions below the theme Empowerment of 
Type 2 diabetics aren’t technology related, therefore they aren’t included in the POV. Finally, 
concerning the theme Technology solution for Type 2 diabetics, the first category Technology 
promoting healthy grocery shopping concerning the amount of information and functionalities as 
well as the type of technology isn’t selected to be incorporated in the POV. The reason being that 
this will be explored in the co-design workshop. 
The second category below the theme Technology solution for Type 2 diabetics includes all the 
functionalities maybe to include. Since they aren’t of high relevance for both focus group 
participants, they aren’t included in the POV. The sixth functionality Social networking is related 
to the seventh challenge Lack of understanding from social network in the category Challenges of 
type 2 diabetics, therefore this challenge is as well deselected to include. In addition, the second 
challenge below Grocery shopping challenges, Expensive diabetes-friendly foods and medicine, 
isn’t included, because a functionality to solve this being discounts and coupons was categorized 
by the focus group participants in the second exercise as not of relevance. 
The only functionality deselected even though it’s categorized as a functionality to include is Food 
diary incl. goal setting. One reason for this is that there already exist many threats on the market 
since Food diary in table 3 is found to be the fifth most common characteristic in the identified 
mobile applications. Another threat is the CGM system Veri mentioned by the second focus group 
participant. This system involves a mobile application, which is connected to a sensor constantly 
==PAGE:80==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
76 
 
measuring the blood sugar level. Images of consumed foods can be uploaded in the application, 
and it can afterwards show the influence of these foods on the blood sugar level (Veri, n.d.). After 
these deselections, the point-of-view (POV) is concluded to be the following:  
Type 2 diabetics are different and responsible for their own health, therefore they need a 
personalized technology, which provides nutritional information and information of how foods 
influence their blood sugar. 
Therefore, the POV consists of the three following needs: 
1. The need for personalization. 
2. The need for nutritional information. 
3. The need for knowledge of how foods influence the blood sugar. 
These needs are combined with several insights. The first need is included in the POV for two 
reasons. Firstly, because it from the analysis of the focus group is found that type 2 diabetics are 
different represented by the theme Differences among Type 2 diabetics. Secondly, the need is 
included since the analysis resulted in that the subcategory Personalization is placed in the category 
containing the functionalities to include in a technology.  
The second need in the POV is based on the finding from the focus analysis that type 2 diabetics 
need to be empowered, because they are responsible for their own health, which constitutes the 
fourth subcategory below the theme Empowerment of Type 2 diabetics. This responsibility is 
related to the first challenge Difficult changing to healthy habits in the category Grocery shopping 
challenges. In addition, the need is included due to the identified challenge Insufficient nutrition 
labels below the category Grocery shopping challenges. Furthermore, Access nutritional 
information and the connected Understanding of quantity are functionalities categorized as some 
to include in a technology. 
Lastly, the third need in the POV is incorporated, because two of the challenges in the category 
Challenges of type 2 diabetics are Lack of knowledge of factors influencing blood sugar and Lack 
of knowledge of carbohydrates. In addition, the third technology-based patient empowerment 
intervention below the theme Empowerment of Type 2 diabetics is Obtaining knowledge of 
influencing factors from glucose monitor. Furthermore, Scanning of groceries to obtain influence 
on blood sugar is categorized a functionality to include. Related to this scanning functionality is 
==PAGE:81==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
77 
 
the Goal setting functionality, which as well is in the category of those to include in a technology 
for type 2 diabetics. This functionality involves individual goals for the blood sugar level. Overall, 
the POV creates the basis for the last three phases in the Design Thinking process (Hasso Plattner 
Institute of Design, 2010). 
 
5. Ideate, Prototype & Test: Co-design workshop 
In the Design Thinking process, the third phase is Ideate, the fourth is Prototype, and the fifth is 
Test (The Interaction Design Foundation, n.d.). These are all incorporated in a workshop. 
Workshops including both designers, users, and techniques such as prototypes are a central method 
within Participatory Design (Robertson & Simonsen, 2012). These workshops are called co-design 
workshops, where the users as experts of their experience have an active role in idea generation 
and development of concepts. These activities are performed by the use of tools provided by the 
researcher (Sanders & Stappers, 2008).  
The researcher and designer can be the same person, and this person has the facilitator role. In this 
role, the person collaborates with the users during the activities and at the same time encourage 
the creativity of the users (Sanders & Stappers, 2008). In the co-design workshop of this project, 
the users are type 2 diabetics, and the facilitator is me, who is both the researcher and the designer. 
Overall, the co-design workshop is used as a method, because it can include both idea generation 
from Ideate phase, prototyping from the Prototype phase, and test from the Test phase (Hasso 
Plattner Institute of Design, 2010).  
 
5.1 Planning of Co-design workshop 
Prior to the co-design workshop, a guide found in Appendix 14 was created. The first part of the 
guide is an introduction, while the last part is an ending. In the ending, the participants are thanked 
for their participation. Through the introduction, information is given to the participants, and a part 
of it should be the purpose of the study (Halkier, 2015). The first part of the introduction the co-
design workshop guide is the presentation of this project’s purpose. A round for the participants 
to introduce themselves is as well relevant to include in the introduction (Halkier, 2015). In the 
co-design workshop guide, a round of introduction constitutes the second part of the introduction.  
==PAGE:82==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
78 
 
In the introduction, it’s as well important to describe the focus and the format (Halkier, 2015). The 
third part of the introduction in the co-design workshop guide consists of two points, where the 
first is an explanation of the focus of the workshop. The focus is to design a technology, which 
can promote healthy grocery shopping habits in supermarkets for type 2 diabetics. This relates to 
the problem statement of this project. The second point describes the format of the co-design 
workshop. This description includes the duration, information about the sound recording and a 
screen recording, as well as an explanation of the pictures captured. In addition, the two activities 
are briefly described including the number of participants and my role. After the introduction, the 
two activities included in the co-design workshop, brainwalking and thinking aloud test, are 
described in the guide in Appendix 14. Figure 24 gives an overview of the guide. 
 
 
Figure 24: The structure of the co-design workshop guide. 
==PAGE:83==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
79 
 
Brainwalking is a method, which is a version of the classical brainstorming, where all participants 
are asked to contribute with an idea, and afterwards the idea is developed further by the other 
participants. They can add additions to the original idea or come up with new ideas based on the 
idea (Københavns Universitet, n.d.-a). Figure 24 shows that this definition constitutes the first part 
of the brainwalking description in the co-design workshop guide in Appendix 14. 
The second part below the brainwalking description in the guide concerns the seven rules for the 
activity, which are the same rules as for a brainstorming. These rules are the following: develop 
others’ ideas further, don’t judge each other’s ideas, focus on quantity, listen to each other’s ideas, 
crazy ideas are welcome, be visual, and stay focused on the problem (Københavns Universitet, 
n.d.-a). This problem is mentioned in the third part of the brainwalking description in the guide, 
which as seen from figure 24 contains all the instructions for the brainwalking activity. In some 
brainwalking activities, the participants are asked to formulate the problem and choose the best 
formulation (Københavns Universitet, n.d.-a). However, the problem to focus on in the 
brainwalking in this project was formulated prior to the activity since it’s the problem statement 
of this project seen in the introduction.  
As a part of the instructions, the three rounds of the brainwalking are as well described. The first 
round is an individual idea generation, where all participants should write one idea on a paper 
(Københavns Universitet, n.d.-a). The participants get 5 minutes to generate this idea, which 
should be described on an A3 paper with a name, some sentences, and a sketch using various pens. 
These materials are seen on an image in Appendix 15. In the following second round, the further 
development of the original ideas take place, and this should be done in silence (Københavns 
Universitet, n.d.-a). The original idea on the A3 papers should be send to the left, therefore an 
original idea is received from the right. Sticky notes and pens seen in Appendix 15 are used for 
the additions to ideas, which can be described with words, symbols, and or drawings (Københavns 
Universitet, n.d.-a). For each original idea, the participants have 2 minutes to add an idea. The 
original ideas from the first round rotate until all participants have developed further on all of them.  
The first and second round of the brainwalking are used for generating ideas being technology 
solutions, which can solve the needs of type 2 diabetics described in the POV in section 4.7. 
Therefore, these two rounds constitute Ideate, which is the third Design Thinking phase (Hasso 
Plattner Institute of Design, 2010). The benefits of brainwalking are that it encourages all to 
==PAGE:84==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
80 
 
participate in the idea generation, and that the participants can use their creativity and knowledge 
for the further development of each other’s ideas (Københavns Universitet, n.d.-a). Therefore, the 
type 2 diabetics can be the experts of their needs related to a technology in the context of grocery 
shopping in supermarkets. At the same time, I as both the researcher and designer can in the 
facilitator role collaborate with them and encourage creativity (Sanders & Stappers, 2008).  
The third round of the brainwalking is as well described in the co-design workshop guide in 
Appendix 14. In this round, the owner gets the A3 paper with the original idea back and has 2 
minutes to read all the sticky notes added by the other participants. After reading, the owner should 
then select the best idea or ideas (Københavns Universitet, n.d.-a). Based on this or these, the 
owner has 5 minutes to sketch a resulting technology using different pens and a blank A4 paper or 
an A4 paper containing three iPhone templates6. In addition, two papers with Material Design 
system icons can be used for inspiration when sketching7 . These materials are seen in Appendix 
15. 
After the sketching, the best united idea visualized through the sketches is presented for the other 
participants. The presented ideas constitute design concepts. The sketches included in the design 
concepts are interactable and low-resolution prototypes based on the technology solution ideas 
generated in the first and second round of the brainwalking. Therefore, the third round constitutes 
the fourth phase Prototype of the Design Thinking process (Hasso Plattner Institute of Design, 
2010). 
As seen on figure 24, the second activity in the co-design workshop being thinking aloud tests is 
started after a short break. These tests represent the fifth and final Design Thinking phase namely 
Test (Hasso Plattner Institute of Design, 2010). These includes the third iteration of the prototype 
of the YourHealthyShopping (YHS) mobile application from the preceding the special course, 
which contains personal discounts and makes personalized nutritional information accessible. 
Everything in the prototype relevant for the test was made interactable prior to the test. A prototype 
is tested in this phase to get feedback and thereby get to know more about the users (Hasso Plattner 
 
6 https://imockups.com/iphone-xs-wireframe-template_3884  
7 https://material.io/design/iconography/system-icons.html#design-principles  
==PAGE:85==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
81 
 
Institute of Design, 2010). This can be obtained via a thinking aloud test, which is a usability test, 
where the test participants use a system while they describe their thoughts (Nielsen, 2012a). 
Three thinking aloud tests are conducted, where the participants one at a time performs the test. In 
the meantime, the others take a break. My role is a test facilitator, and before the tests instructions 
are given. Figure 24 shows that these constitute the first part of the description of the thinking 
aloud tests in the co-design workshop guide in Appendix 14. These instructions are informing 
them about that they aren’t being tested but the prototype is tested, that the purpose is to obtain 
feedback, and that there aren’t any right or wrong answers (Usability.gov, n.d.)(Hasso Plattner 
Institute of Design, 2010).  
The second part about the thinking aloud tests in the guide is an introduction of the prototype and 
the test itself. Regarding the prototype, the participants are explained what a prototype is and that 
they should test the prototype of the mobile application YourHealthyShopping. After this, the 
thinking aloud test is explained. Firstly, the test is defined so the participants know that they should 
describe what they are doing and why, while they are using the prototype (Nielsen, 2012a). 
Secondly, they are informed about that the test consists of six tasks and one question, which should 
be read out loud, and that I will remind them if they forget to think aloud (Usability.gov, 
n.d.)(Nielsen, 2012a). Thirdly, the scenario for the test is described for the participants, which is 
that they are using the mobile application YourHealthyShopping in a supermarket when grocery 
shopping (Hasso Plattner Institute of Design, 2010). Lastly, they are asked if they have any 
questions and are told to start by reading the first task (Usability.gov, n.d.). 
In order to perform the thinking aloud test, tasks should namely be provided to the test participants 
(Nielsen, 2012a). These are therefore printed and placed next to a laptop with the prototype. An 
image in Appendix 15 shows these materials as well as printed consent forms and the co-design 
workshop guide, which supported my facilitator role. Figure 24 shows that the tasks constitute the 
third part in the description of the thinking aloud tests in the co-design workshop guide in 
Appendix 14. The first task is about exploring the home page, whereas the second and third task 
concern the personal discounts. The fourth and fifth task ask the participants to check nutritional 
information of two different groceries One of the groceries has a barcode and is therefore 
scannable, while the other cannot be scanned. In the sixth task, the obtained nutritional information 
==PAGE:86==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
82 
 
should be personalized by removing fibers, which is done in the profile. Finally, the participants 
are asked a question regarding missing elements in the prototype. 
 
5.2 Communication with Participants  
On the 7th of October 2021, the communication about the co-design workshop was sent to the six 
selected participants. This was the fifth email, and it contained a description of the workshop 
including the duration, the purpose, and the activities. In addition, it highlighted the date, time, and 
location as well as informed them about the recordings of the workshop and their anonymous 
appearance in this project. Lastly, the participants received a deadline being the 19th of October 
2021 for confirming their participation in the workshop. The email text is placed in Appendix 10.  
Only one of the selected participants confirmed his participation before the deadline namely on 
the 11th of October 2021. Therefore, the other five selected participants received a reminder email 
on the 20th of October 2021. This reminded them about the exceeded deadline for confirming their 
participation in the workshop as well as highlighted the date, time, and location. In addition, they 
were asked to confirm as soon as possible. Appendix 10 contains the text of this email. After this 
email, one of the selected participants confirmed her participation, while another participant 
informed that he couldn’t participate due to sickness. On the 21st of October 2021, two emails were 
sent to two of the previously deselected type 2 diabetics. One of them responded and confirmed 
his participation on the following day. In addition, one of the selected participants confirmed her 
participation on the 23rd of October 2021.  
One last and seventh email was sent on the 25th of October 2021, which was the day before the co-
design workshop. The two selected participants, who hadn’t confirmed their participation, received 
an email with a reminder about the co-design workshop. However, none of the two participants 
replied. For the four type 2 diabetics, who had confirmed their participation, the email involved a 
friendly reminder about the workshop and as well my contact information. Appendix 10 contains 
the text of the two emails. After these emails were sent, one of the selected participants, who had 
previously confirmed her participation, decided to opt out. Therefore, the final number of 
participants for the co-design workshop was three. Figure 25 shows both the communication and 
the cancellations regarding the workshop.

5.3 Co-design workshop results and analysis 
On Tuesday the 26th of October 2021 from 17:00 PM to approximately 19:00 PM, the co-design 
workshop was completed. Appendix 15 contains images showing the meeting room used for the 
workshop. As mentioned in the previous section, the final number of participants in the workshop 
was three. The first participant and the second participant also participated in the focus group, 
therefore their personal information is described in section 4.4. However, the third participant, who 
is a 59 year old man, didn’t participate in the focus group. He is educated journalist now studying 
to become pedagogue and was diagnosed with type 2 diabetes 5 years ago. In the brainwalking, I 
as both a researcher and designer participated, and I am therefore referred to as the fourth 
participant. Therefore, the co-design workshop ended up having one more participant than the 
recommended for a co-design workshop team (Gilbert, 2016).  
 
5.3.1 Ideas from Brainwalking 
As described in section 5.1, the co-design workshop involved two activities. The first activity was 
the brainwalking. Afterwards, all four participants’ original individual ideas from the first round 
and the further development in the second round were photographed. The first participant’s 
==PAGE:88==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021

original idea is a mobile application containing meal plans, shopping lists, calorie counter, and 
blood sugar data from a glucose monitor.  
Afterwards, the second participant has added that the meal plans could include easy and cheap 
dishes, and as well that the calorie counter could include amount of carbohydrates. The additions 
of the fourth participant are that the meal plans could be based on chosen recipes, which should be 
suggested on the basis of the blood sugar level and possible to add to a shopping list. Finally, the 
suggestion of the third participant are to add a climate impact measurer in the mobile application.

The second participant’s idea is as well a mobile application based on a CGM being FreeStyle 
Libre. The purpose of the application is for type 2 diabetics to understand their carbohydrate 
intolerance. With the mobile application, they should be able to scan groceries in supermarkets 
and thereby obtain the induvial effect on the blood sugar. Following this scanning, alternative 
groceries with less carbohydrates could be suggested. In addition, recipes and related shopping 
lists are suggested based on the carbohydrate intolerance.  
In the second round, the fourth participant has added three ideas, where the first is to color code 
the scanned groceries. Green being good effect on blood sugar, yellow representing medium effect, 
and red showing bad effect. The second idea is that nutritional information could be obtained after 
scanning, and the third is that it should be possible to edit the recipes and shopping lists. 
Afterwards, the third participant has added that climate impact could be shown after scanning of 
groceries. Lastly, the first participant has just stated his support of the second participant’s original 
idea. Figure 27 shows this original idea and the ideas added in the second round.

In the first round of the brainwalking, the third participant has created three ideas instead of one. 
The first original idea is called Help-cart, which is a smart shopping cart with a screen and 
connected to the internet. This screen shows where to find the best groceries. In the second round, 
the first participant has suggested to add a barcode reader on the shopping cart. Afterwards, the 
second participant has asked if the physical location of the groceries are shown on the screen and 
if best groceries means best alternatives. Lastly, the fourth participant has added a question 
regarding what the selection of the best groceries is based on.

in mobile applications e.g., the Coop application in order to guide the customers the best groceries.

fourth participant has asked what the shopping lists are based on and suggested that the basis could 
be selections or recipes.  
The third and last original idea of the third participant is a game in a mobile application, where the 
user is rewarded with power if the right groceries are purchased. In the second round, the first 
participant hasn’t added any ideas. However, the second participant has expressed her support of 
the idea since it makes grocery shopping a positive game. Finally, one idea from the fourth

The original idea of the fourth participant is a mobile application named MyHealthyShopping. It 
contains a personalized profile, where type 2 diabetics can add foods by separating them into three 
color categories depending on how they affect the blood sugar. The green category represents 
good, the orange represents medium, and the red represents not good. This idea of the color 
categories is inspired by the warning labels described by Gupta et al. 2020. In the profile, the 
wanted nutritional information can as well be added. This information and the color category are 
obtained when scanning groceries with the mobile application while shopping in supermarkets.

barcode in the product declaration on groceries, which can be scanned to get the most important 
information. Lastly, the additions of the second participant are to have alternatives when a grocery

To conclude, the ideas arising during the first round of the brainwalking are mainly mobile 
applications being 5 out of 6. The mobile application by the first, second, and fourth participant is 
personalized. The personalized mobile application by the first participant and the second 
participant both involve blood sugar data from a glucose monitor. As well, both of these mobile 
applications include shopping lists. The third participant’s second original idea is also a mobile 
application including shopping lists.  
Regarding the mentioned personalization, the mobile application of the fourth participant is 
involving a profile, which includes blood sugar since the foods can be categorized based on their 
effect on the blood sugar level. In addition, this mobile application includes the possibility to obtain 
nutritional information of scanned groceries. A scanning functionality is also in the mobile 
application of the second participant to get the blood sugar effect of groceries. Furthermore, the 
most common added ideas in the second round of the brainwalking are discounts, healthy 
alternatives, climate impact, and shopping lists based on recipes.  
 
5.3.2 Design concepts from Brainwalking 
After the third and final round of the brainwalking, the result is four design concepts, which were 
photographed. Each picture includes the original idea from the first round, the added ideas in the 
second round, and the final design concept from the third round. These pictures were uploaded to 
a Miro board as four different collected design concepts, and afterwards sticky notes describing 
these were added. The descriptions on the sticky notes are based on the presentations of the design 
concepts in the third round of the brainwalking, which were sound recorded. A picture of all the 
four design concepts and a link to access them on the Miro board are placed in Appendix 16.  
The design concept of the first participant is a mobile application containing a profile, meal plans 
and shopping lists. The profile contains name, weight, diet group, and blood sugar levels. Blood 
sugar levels are obtained from a glucose monitor and are visualized as numbers from today, 
yesterday, and the last year. The first participant has stated that these blood sugar levels are 
important for type 2 diabetics to purchase the appropriate groceries in supermarkets. Finally, the 
meal plans in the mobile application are suggested on the basis of the weight and blood sugar 
levels in the profile. In addition, the meal plans contains recipes, and based on these recipes, 
==PAGE:95==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
91 
 
shopping lists are automatically created. Figure 30 shows the first design concept, which was 
developed by the first participant.  
 
 
Figure 30: The first design concept created by the first participant.  
 
The second participant has created a design concept of a mobile application named My Blood 
Sugar Shopping. First of all, this is based on a continuous blood sugar measuring by a CGM 
system, and this measurement is visualized in the application via a graph. The graph gives an 
overview of how various foods affect the blood sugar, and from this the carbohydrate intolerance 
can be defined. Secondly, the application contains a blood sugar profile represented by a letter and 
containing a scanning functionality. This scanning functionality can be used to scan the barcodes 
of groceries when shopping in supermarkets.  
After a scan, the result is the influence of a grocery on the blood sugar level with the corresponding 
color, where green represents low effect, yellow is medium effect, and red represents high effect. 
As well, the scanning result involves the tolerated amount of the specific grocery and a healthier 
alternative with a specific location in the supermarket. Likewise, payment should be possible. 
==PAGE:96==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
92 
 
Lastly, the mobile application suggests recipes either based on the blood sugar profile or the 
scanned groceries, and a collection of these recipes can be created in a meal plan. In addition, the 
shopping lists are automatically generated on the basis of the recipes. Figure 31 contains the design 
concept of the second participant constituting the second design concept. 
 
 
Figure 31: The second design concept created by the second participant.  
 
The third participant’s design concept isn’t based on any of his three original ideas from the first 
round of the brainwalking or any of the added ideas from the other participants in the second round. 
Instead, the design concept is an add on named Measure Me! for the Coop mobile application. The 
third participant has argued to use the existing Coop application since it’s well-functioning and 
contains a profile. In addition, it’s according to him challenging to create a mobile application, 
which can be used in all supermarkets e.g., due to their different discounts.  
First of all, the profile in the Coop application should be updated so the users can add health data 
e.g., weight and preferred foods and as well goals e.g., lower blood sugar. The add on itself 
involves a scanning functionality with the purpose to scan groceries in supermarkets. By a scan, 
==PAGE:97==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
93 
 
the influence on the intake of macronutrients e.g., protein and fat can be obtained. This influence 
is visualized via the add on in different graphs, which include a green zone being good and a red 
zone being bad. A graph can be in real time or showing the last week, month, or year. Figure 32 
contains the third design concept, which is created by the third participant.  
 
 
Figure 32: The third design concept created by the third participant.  
 
Lastly, the design concept of the fourth participant is a mobile application named 
MyHealthyShopping. It contains three functionalities being a personalized profile, a scanning 
functionality, and personalized discounts. In the profile, three different information types can be 
filled out. The first type is personal information being name, age, and gender. The second type is 
food preferences, where foods can be added and afterwards viewed in the three color categories, 
which are green, yellow, and red. The green category should contain foods with low effect on 
blood sugar, and foods with high effect should be placed in the red category. The third information 
type in the profile is nutritional information, and the nutritional information obtained when 
scanning groceries can be selected and edited. 
==PAGE:98==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
94 
 
The barcodes of groceries are scanned by using the scanning functionality. The result of a scanning 
involves the selected color category in the profile for the specific grocery, the personalized 
nutritional information of the grocery, and as well a link to a healthier alternative being a grocery 
placed in the green category in the profile. This category is as well the basis for the personalized 
discounts in the mobile application. Each discount can be opened in order to read the description 
of it. Figure 33 shows all the functionalities of the fourth design concept made by the fourth 
participant.  
 
 
Figure 33: The fourth design concept created by the fourth participant.  
 
5.3.3 Affinity diagramming of Design concepts 
After the sticky notes describing the design concepts were added, affinity diagramming of the four 
design concepts were performed. Within UX, Affinity diagramming is a method, which can be 
used to organize ideas found during a research study. The previously mentioned Miro board was 
used for the affinity diagramming, and it was only performed by me as a designer. However, the 
method involved the two usual steps overall. First, sticky notes were generated based on the sticky 
==PAGE:99==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
95 
 
notes with the descriptions of the design concepts, and some sticky notes could even be reused. 
Each idea related to the design concepts was added to a separate sticky note (Pernice, 2018).  
Afterwards, the notes were organized into overall categories being different functionalities and the 
type of technology. The notes in these categories were then again sorted into subcategories if the 
notes were describing different ideas (Pernice, 2018). Appendix 16 includes a picture of the overall 
result of the affinity diagramming and a link for the Miro board. The color of the sticky notes 
indicates which participant suggested the idea. Green represents the first participant, purple is the 
second participant, red represents the third participant, and blue is the fourth participant. In total, 
7 categories are created during the affinity diagramming. Table 14 contains these categories and 
their subcategories.  
 
Table 14: Overview of the categories and subcategories from the affinity diagramming.
