5.3.4 Results and Analysis of Thinking aloud tests 
As described in section 5.1, the second activity in the co-design workshop was the three thinking 
aloud tests of the prototype of the mobile application YourHealthyShopping. After the tests, a table 
was created on the Miro board, where the columns represent each of the three participants, and the 
rows represent resp. the tasks and the final question. In addition, a row is added for an extra 
question, which two of the participants were asked. Appendix 16 contains a picture of the table 
and a link to the Miro board.  
Overall, the result of a thinking aloud test is qualitative data being findings from the participants’ 
assessments of the prototype (Budiu, 2017). In the table, the fields related to the tasks contain a 
description of how the participants completed each task based on the sound recording and screen 
recording of the tests. If the participants received assistance or an explanation related to a task, this 
is indicated with the first two words of the sentence being bolded text.  
==PAGE:106==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
102 
 
As well, severity rating of the findings was performed in order to identify and prioritize usability 
strengths and problems (Budiu, 2017). For the findings from the thinking aloud tests, the severity 
rating is based on how easy the tasks was solved by the participant. Hearts in three different colors 
are used as the rating scale and are placed in the fields in the table. The green heart indicates that 
the specific task isn’t a usability problem since it was easy to solve for the participant. The yellow 
heart means that the participant struggled a bit, and therefore the task reveals a minor usability 
problem. Finally, the red heart indicates that it took several tries for the participant to solve the 
task, which therefore constitutes a major usability problem that should be prioritized as the first to 
be fixed (Nielsen, 1994). Furthermore, the fields in the table for the questions contains the 
participants’ comments from the sound recording. In addition, some fields include green text, 
which are suggestions or feedback provided by the participants. Appendix 16 contains a picture of 
the table including the descriptions, severity ratings, comments, and suggestions as well as a link 
to access the table on the Miro board. 
The first task concerns the content of the home page, and the second participant understood that 
this includes the groceries scanned lately and personalized discounts. Therefore, the second 
participant solved the task with ease. The discounts were as well understood by the third 
participant, and he also mentioned that it would be possible to swipe in the lists. However, he 
misunderstood the purpose of the scan since he thought that it was for purchase. His solution is 
therefore marked with a yellow heart. Finally, the first participant didn’t understand the content of 
the home page, which is indicated with the red heart. Despite this, he contributed with two 
suggestions, where the first is to replace the activate buttons for the personal discounts with buttons 
directing to information about the discounts. The second suggestion is to add advertising circulars 
from supermarkets. 
The solutions to the second task regarding how to check the personal discounts are marked with 
resp. red and yellow hearts. The second and third participant’s solution are marked with a yellow 
since they both first tried to click the title “Your discounts” on the home page. Afterwards, the 
second participant mentioned that she would swipe through the list of discounts. In the end, they 
pressed the discount icon in the menu directing to the page with the personal discount. However, 
the second task was challenging for the first participant. He thought that he could find the discounts 
in the profile and that the scan icon in the menu would direct him to the home page. He ended up 
==PAGE:107==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
103 
 
clicking many of the icons in the menu several times before he found the Your discounts page. 
Therefore, he suggested to replace the minus icon in the menu for this page with a green “v”, 
because the minus according to him doesn’t symbolize discounts.  
The third task concerning how to activate the personal discounts was easily solved by all three 
participants by clicking the activate buttons. These fields in the table are therefore all including a 
green heart. The third participant even understood how to deactivate discounts. Likewise, this 
participant found it easy to solve the fourth task, which is about scanning the barcode of an iced 
coffee in order to obtain its amount of carbohydrates. He understood that the white scanning field 
in reality would be the camera showing the barcode, however in the prototype, the white field 
should be clicked, therefore he was told this.  
This help was also given to the first participant, who didn’t understand the purpose of the scanning 
field and instead thought that the nutritional information would appear in this field. In addition, he 
selected the search icon in the menu as the first try in this task. After this, he clicked the scan icon, 
which he however suggested to change so it includes a barcode. Since this task caused the first 
participant a lot of confusion, his solution is marked with a red heart in the table. Like the first 
participant, the second participant pressed the search icon in the menu as her first try, because she 
thought that this would give her the opportunity to search for the iced coffee. After this try, she 
solved the task without any problems, therefore her solution in the table has a yellow heart.  
One of the solutions to the fifth task with the purpose to search for an apple in order to get the 
nutritional information is as well marked with a yellow heart. This is the solution by the first 
participant since he clicked the question mark on the Scan & Search page before clicking the search 
field on the page. The two other participants only tried to use the search field, and they both 
understood that an apple cannot be scanned. Therefore, the fields in the table containing their 
solutions include a green heart indicating that they easily solved the task.  
Furthermore, the first and third participant were asked an extra question about what the question 
mark on the Scan & Search page could be used for. The first participant thought that clicking on 
the question mark would result in a drop-down with a list of foods and that the search field 
therefore only should be used for foods not included in the list. This is marked a suggestion in the 
table, and the third participant as well had a suggestion. He suggested to replace the question mark 
with a paper clip, even though he understood that a pop-up with text would appear from the 
==PAGE:108==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
104 
 
question mark. The content of the pop-up, which is a help text about that the search field can be 
used for foods without a barcode, was explained to both participants. 
The sixth and final task concerning how to remove fibers from the nutritional information obtained 
after scanning of or searching for a grocery. This task was easily solved by the second and third 
participant by going directly to the profile in the prototype, therefore a green heart is placed for 
their solutions in the table. However, the solution by the first participant is marked with a red heart 
since he struggled to find where the nutritional information can be edited. He visited the Your 
discounts page twice before he realized that the task should be solved in the profile.  
The last row in the table accessible in Appendix 16 contains the comments and suggestions from 
the participants after they were asked about missing elements in the prototype. The suggestions 
from the first participant are to include a pedometer for counting burned calories during grocery 
shopping in supermarkets and as well to add two fields in the profile. One of the fields is for a diet 
group incorporated in his design concept. Opposite, the second participants doesn’t think this 
should be added, because type 2 diabetics use diets differently. The other field suggested by the 
first participants is for the three colored blood glucose effect categories from the fourth 
participant’s design concept. 
As well, the second participant has suggested some additions to the profile. According to her, it 
should be possible to add the tolerated amounts of macronutrients in groceries e.g., carbohydrates. 
In the Nutrients info window, different types of sweeteners inspired by a table on the website 
Diabetes 2 Danmark should be included8. Opposite, the third participant has suggested to remove 
information from the profile namely gender. According to him, the reasons are that this 
information doesn’t matter today and isn’t relevant for type 2 diabetics in the context of grocery 
shopping. Furthermore, a suggestion made by the third participant is to add a payment page, so it’s 
possible to see the amount of money spend during grocery shopping and to pay at the end.  
According to the second participant a calculation functionality should be added so it can be 
calculated how much of a specific grocery that can be consumed before reaching the intake of 5 
grams carbohydrates. Likewise, links directing the type 2 diabetics to diabetes-friendly recipes on 
 
8 https://diabetes2danmark.dk/onewebmedia/Soedestoffer.pdf  
==PAGE:109==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
105 
 
the website Madbanditten should be included9. A last element to include according to the second 
participant is blood sugar data. This data can be transferred from the health mobile application on 
iPhone or the mobile application Veri, which is a part of a CGM system (Veri, n.d.). During the 
focus group, she mentioned that she uses this system. 
 
5.4 Conclusion on Co-design workshop 
Based on the affinity diagramming of the four design concepts, it’s clear that the technology to 
solve the three needs described in the POV in section 4.7 is a mobile application. 3 out of 4 
participants created a design of a mobile application. In this section, it will be concluded which 
functionalities to include and not include in this mobile application. These conclusions will be 
made on the basis on the characteristics present in many mobile applications in table 3, the three 
needs in the POV in section 4.7, the results from the affinity diagramming in section 5.3.3, and as 
well the findings from the thinking aloud tests in section 5.3.4. Table 15 contains all the categories 
and subcategories from the affinity diagramming mentioned as functionalities. As well, it’s stated 
if these will or will not be included in the next iteration of the prototype with related arguments.

The next iteration of the prototype should first of all contain a profile since it will ensure a 
personalized mobile application and therefore solve the first need in the POV in section 4.7, which 
is about personalization. In addition, the affinity diagramming shows that a profile is incorporated 
in the design concepts by all four participants. The profile should contain both personal 
information and health data. In two of the design concepts, personal information is included. Below 
personal information, name and date of birth seem to be basics to include since none of the 
participants commented on them in the thinking aloud test. However, the third participant has 
highlighted the irrelevance of gender, therefore it should be removed.  
The health data in the profile should include nutritional information since this is the second need 
in the POV. This information is in the profile of the prototype of the mobile application 
YourHealthyShopping tested in the thinking aloud tests. In these tests, the information was easy 
to find for 2 out of 3 participants. However, it’s suggested by the second participant to include 
types of sweeteners from the website Diabetes 2 Danmark in the information10. In addition, in the 
focus group the second participant mentioned that the amount of calories is important for the 
diabetes control of some type 2 diabetics. Therefore, energy should be included in the nutritional 
information. As well, protein should be added since the third participant included this 
macronutrient in his design concept.  
Overall, the possibility to choose different nutritional information is in accordance with the 
differences among type 2 diabetics, which is stated in the POV in section 4.7. Furthermore, 
allergies is like the nutritional information placed in the profile of the prototype of 
YourHealthyShopping. The information concerning allergies wasn’t mentioned by any of the three 
participants during the thinking aloud tests and as well none of them have included this information 
in the profile of their design concepts. Therefore, allergies don’t seem relevant and should be 
removed from the profile.  
Opposite, food preferences should be incorporated in the profile since 3 out of 4 design concepts 
contain some kind of this information. Three color categories indicating if the foods have low, 
medium, high effect on the blood glucose effect are incorporated in both the design concept of the 
second and fourth participant. The only difference is the creation of them. As well, in the thinking 
 
10 https://diabetes2danmark.dk/onewebmedia/Soedestoffer.pdf 
==PAGE:112==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
108 
 
aloud test the first participant has mentioned to add the three color categories. The categories are 
relevant since they can solve the need third need in the POV in section 4.7 about understanding 
foods’ effect on the blood sugar. Related to this need is blood sugar levels, which are incorporated 
in the context of a profile in 2 out of 4 design concepts. Opposite, weight in the profile isn’t relevant 
for the POV. 
According to 2 out of 4 participants, the blood sugar levels should be measured by a glucose 
monitor. In the thinking aloud test, the second participant even suggested that the blood sugar data 
could be transferred from other mobile applications such as Veri, which is a part of a CGM system 
(Veri, n.d.). In addition, the 2 of the design concepts contain different visualizations of the blood 
sugar level. The second participant has suggested a graph as visualization, while the first 
participant suggested numbers. Both visualizations should therefore be included in the next 
iteration of the prototype. Related to these visualizations and thereby the third need in the POV 
are goals concerning blood sugar level, which are included in the profile of the third participant’s 
design concept. 
Other than a profile, the prototype should include the scanning functionality for barcodes of 
groceries. The main reason being that it can be a part of the solution for the third need in the POV 
about obtaining knowledge of foods’ influence on the blood sugar. As well, the scanning 
functionality is incorporated in 3 out of 4 design concepts, and the scanning functionality was 
fairly easy to use for 2 out of 3 participants in the thinking aloud tests. In addition, it shouldn’t be 
problematic that Scan and or search for foods is found to be the characteristic present in third most 
of the identified mobile applications in table 3. The reason being that it’s a basic functionality.  
The result of a scanning should be nutritional information and blood sugar effect. To obtain blood 
sugar effect and to get nutritional information are two of the needs in the POV in section 4.7. As 
well, both scanning results are included in 2 out of 4 design concepts. As previously mentioned, 
both the second and fourth participant have incorporated three color categories for the blood sugar 
effect. The second participant has as well incorporated the tolerated amount of the specific grocery 
in the scanning result, which should be included. The last element in a scanning result should be a 
healthier alternative, which is in 2 out of 4 design concepts. Especially, the suggestion by the fourth 
participant of a personalized healthier alternative from the green color category is relevant. 
==PAGE:113==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
109 
 
Lastly, a payment functionality is included in the design concept of the second participant and is 
suggested by the third participant during the thinking aloud test. However, this functionality isn’t 
included in the POV in section 4.7. The same goes for meal plans and shopping lists, which the 
first and second participant have incorporated in their design concepts. In addition, Shopping lists 
is in table 3 shown to be the characteristic that most of the identified mobile applications contain, 
therefore there are many threats on the market. To sum up, the next iteration of the prototype 
shouldn’t involve payment, meal plans, and shopping lists. 
Likewise, personal discounts shouldn’t be included in the prototype. The reasons being that they 
aren’t included in the POV and that only the fourth participant, which is me as a designer, has 
incorporated them in the design concept. However, 2 out of 4 design concepts contain recipes. The 
second participant has incorporated recipes as suggestions based on the information in the profile 
or the scanned groceries. As well, she has mentioned some recipes from a website in the thinking 
aloud test11. Therefore, the recipes could be a solution to the first need in the POV in section 4.7 
about personalization, and they could be presented in a scanning result. 
 
6. Prototype & Test: Prototyping and Expert Review 
After the co-design workshop including both ideation, prototyping, and testing, another iteration 
of resp. the Prototype phase and the Test phase are performed since the Design Thinking process 
is an iterative process (Hasso Plattner Institute of Design, 2010)(The Interaction Design 
Foundation, n.d.). During the Prototype phase, a new iteration of the prototype of the mobile 
application YourHealthyShopping is created based on the conclusions in section 5.4. The 
functionalities concluded as some to include can solve the needs in the POV in section 4.7 and are 
therefore incorporated in the new iteration of the prototype. Afterwards, this iteration is tested in 
the Test phase via an expert review (Harley, 2018). 
 
 
11 https://www.madbanditten.dk/opskrifter/ 
==PAGE:114==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
110 
 
6.1 Creation of Fourth Iteration 
The new iteration of the prototype is the fourth iteration since it’s based on the third iteration of 
YourHealthyShopping. This third iteration is an interactive prototype created in the software 
Adobe XD in the preceding special course, and it’s accessed via the link placed in the co-design 
workshop guide in Appendix 14. Likewise, the fourth iteration is an interactive prototype designed 
in Adobe XD, which is accessed via the link in Appendix 17. Opposite the interactable sketches 
included in the design concepts, an interactive prototype is high-fidelity (Hasso Plattner Institute 
of Design, 2010)(Klein, 2013). This type of prototype supports the process of identifying design 
problems and is appropriate for testing (Klein, 2013).  
All the design specific changes made from the third to the fourth iteration of an interactive 
prototype are found in a table in Appendix 18. This table constitutes a change log and contains 
footnotes explaining the background for the changes. The change log design related, therefore it 
doesn’t include all the additional features in the fourth iteration. All these are included in table 16, 
which gives an overview of the main changes from the third to the fourth iteration since the 
changes are separated into the categories Features, Information, Visuals, and Other.

Below the Other category in table 16, the first change is the name of the mobile application, which 
is changed from YourHealthyShopping to MyGlucoseShopping. This change is inspired by the 
name of the second participant’s design concept, My Blood Sugar Shopping. “Your” is changed 
to “My” since this more clearly emphasizes the personalization, which is the first need in the POV 
in section 4.7. In addition, “Healthy” is replaced with “Glucose” because this specifies that the 
mobile application supports grocery shopping based on the knowledge of how groceries affect the 
blood glucose level, which is the third need in the POV. In addition, an application icon containing 
the name of the fourth iteration is created and seen on figure 41.  
 
 
Figure 41: The application icon for the fourth iteration. 
 
Another change in the Other category in table 16 is that the prototype is designed for another 
iPhone size. The third iteration is designed for an iPhone 12 Pro, while the fourth iteration is a 
design for iPhone 6, 7, 8, and SE. These are smallest sized iPhones that are still used, therefore 
this will make sure that the design fit them. Another change in the same category is the addition 
of a small bar in the top navigation bar.

was Yu Gothic and now is Segoe UI since this style gives a softer look. Another change in this 
category is the color of the background, which is changed from a blue color to a more natural color 
being a light grey. As well, the new primary color of top navigation bar is identified using Material

6.2 Planning of Expert Review 
An expert review is an inspection of a system such as an application completed by usually one UX 
expert. The UX expert reviewing the system should have much knowledge of usability research 
and practices but shouldn’t have contributed to the design process of the system being reviewed. 
The expert review can be performed in any stage of a design process, therefore a high-fidelity 
prototype can as well be reviewed. In addition, the review is a useful supplement to usability 
testing. Usability testing will result in the identification of issues based on the needs and knowledge 
==PAGE:128==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
124 
 
of the users, while an expert review will result in identified minor and major usability problems 
(Harley, 2018). 
Overall, the purpose of an expert review is to identify usability problems and usability strengths 
of a design. The design is evaluated on the basis of the usability heuristics, other usability 
guidelines or principles, and usability research. The result of an expert review is usually a written 
document containing a list of usability problems and a list of usability strengths. The list of 
identified usability strengths should include a description. Meanwhile, the list of identified 
usability problems should contain a bit more information. First of all, a description specifying and 
referencing the usability heuristics, guidelines, principles, and or research that the problems isn’t 
compliant with. This reference could be the UX expert’s own experience.  
In addition, for each usability problem on the list, the UX expert should provide a severity rating 
being High, Medium, or Low. This rating will support the designer in the prioritization for the 
redesign of the system. Furthermore, the list should include one or more recommendations 
specifying how each of the usability problems can be solved. These recommendations can be a 
concrete way to fix it or a need for other types of user research. Finally, examples of best usability 
practices to support these recommendations should be provided by the UX expert (Harley, 2018). 
Prior to the expert review of the high-fidelity prototype of the mobile application 
MyGlucoseShopping, a document containing two pages was prepared. The content of this 
document is seen in Appendix 19. The first page contains a table, where the UX expert should add 
the identified usability problems and as well a guide describing how to fill out the table. The table 
consists of three columns. The first is for descriptions, the second is for severity ratings, and the 
third is for recommendations. It was decided not to include a column for examples of best practices 
in respect of the UX expert’s time. 
In the first column, the UX expert is asked to add a screenshot and description of where the 
usability problem appears in the prototype as well as a description and reference of the violated 
heuristics, guidelines, principles, or research. In the second column, the UX expert should add 
either High, Medium, or Low. In the third column, one or more recommendations and possibly a 
reference should be provided. Finally, it’s mentioned on the first page that the UX expert can add 
more rows to the table if more than five usability problems are identified in the prototype.  
==PAGE:129==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
125 
 
The second page in the document for the expert review is for the usability strengths identified by 
the UX expert. A table and a guide is as well included on this page. The table contains only one 
column for the descriptions of the usability strengths, and the guide specifies to provide a 
screenshot and description of where it exists in the prototype. Additionally, the UX expert is asked 
to include an explanation of why it’s a usability strength and a related reference. Lastly, the guide 
describes that the UX expert is welcome to insert more rows in the table if needed. 
 
6.3 Results of Expert Review 
The UX expert who reviewed the high-fidelity prototype is a Senior UX Designer. On the 1st of 
December 2021, she received the document containing the two tables, guides, and a link to the 
interactive prototype. The following day, the 2nd of December 2021, she performed the review and 
sent the completed document. Appendix 19 contains screenshots of this document. Overall, the 
UX expert has based her review on the ten usability heuristics for interaction design (Nielsen, 
2020). The first table in the completed document contains four usability problems identified by 
the UX expert. Table 23 contains these problems, short descriptions, the heuristics violated, and 
recommendations. 
 
Table 23: The four usability problems identified by the UX expert and sorted by severity.

The first usability problem in table 23 concerns that the users cannot save and cancel the changes 
made to the main categories e.g., Energy on the Nutritional info page in the profile. The UX expert 
has given this usability problem the severity rating High and highlighted that it’s violating the third 
heuristic User control and freedom. The reason being that the users don’t have the option to undo 
their changes related to the main categories on the Nutritional info page. This missing option can 
cause frustration for the users (Nielsen, 2020). The recommendations of the UX expert, which can 
solve this usability problem, are first of all to remove the Save and Cancel button in the 
confirmation dialogs with the types and to and add these buttons on the Nutritional info page 
instead. 
In table 23, the second usability problem is marked with the severity rating Medium. The problem 
is the fact that the users have to remember where they can find their favorite recipes since the 
recipes only are placed below specific groceries scanned. They both have to remember the grocery 
containing a specific favorite and which recipe it’s in the list of recipes. This isn’t compliant with 
the sixth heuristic Recognition rather than recall, because the users have to remember where 
information are located. This recalling of the users should be reduced by making the recipes visible 
and thereby recognizable (Nielsen, 2020). In order to do this, the UX expert has recommended to 
place the recipes in the main navigation or as a level below another item in the menu. Another 
recommendation is to make the users able to save their favorite recipes. 
The third usability problem in table 23 is about inconsistency and is rated as Low on the severity 
rating scale by the UX expert. The first inconsistency is the button text labels in the profile, where 
the buttons below the Shopping tab have the text labels “View” and “Edit”, while the button text 
labels below the Glucose tab are “Graphs” and “Goals”. Regarding this usability problem, the 
recommendation is to rename the button text label “Goals” to “Edit goals”. The second 
==PAGE:131==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
127 
 
inconsistency is that Save and Cancel buttons are used for changes on the Nutritional info page, 
but these buttons don’t appear after changes on the Blood glucose goals page. No recommendation 
for this inconsistency is added by the UX expert. However, both inconsistencies aren’t compliant 
with the fourth heuristic Consistency and standards, because different words and actions are used 
for the same scenarios. This causes that the users have to learn these, which may lead to an 
increased cognitive load (Nielsen, 2020). 
The fourth and last usability problem in table 23 has like the previous usability problem received 
the severity rating Low by the UX expert. This usability problem concerns the See all button on 
the home page of the interactive prototype. The button gives the users the false expectation that all 
the latest scanned groceries will be seen by pressing it. However, the users are directed to the 
Latest scan page that as well contains a See all button, which the users then have to press in order 
to view all the latest scanned groceries. This usability problem violates the fourth heuristic 
Consistency and standards since other applications don’t set false expectations. Therefore, there 
isn’t external consistency (Nielsen, 2020). The recommendation from the UX expert, which can 
solve this problem, is to remove the See all button on the Latest scans page.  
Furthermore, the second table in the document completed by the UX expert contains three 
identified usability strengths. Table 24 contains these strengths, short descriptions, and related 
usability heuristics.  
 
Table 24: The three usability strengths identified by the UX expert. 
Usability 
strength 
Short description 
Heuristic 
 
1 
The feedback message “Your changes have been 
saved” makes it clear that this action is successfully 
performed on the Nutritional info page 
#1 Visibility of system status 
2 
The bottom navigation bar and the Back buttons in 
the top navigation bar are consistent with those in 
other mobile applications 
#4 Consistency and standards 
3 
The top area, the button design, and the titles within 
the interactive prototype are consistent 
#4 Consistency and standards 
 
==PAGE:132==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
128 
 
The first usability strength in table 24 is the feedback message appearing when the users have 
pressed the Save button in one of the confirmation dialogs with the types e.g., Types of sweeteners 
on the Nutritional info page. This strength is related to the first heuristic Visibility of system status 
since the users with this feedback message are informed about the system’s state being that the 
changes are successfully saved (Nielsen, 2020). Furthermore, the second usability strength in table 
24 concerns that the bottom navigation bar and the Back buttons in the top navigation bar are 
consistent with other mobile applications. This is called external consistency and is described in 
the fourth heuristic Consistency and standards. By having external consistency, the users don’t 
have to learn a new navigation and thereby the cognitive load is decreased (Nielsen, 2020). 
In table 24, the third and final usability strength is that the top area including the top navigation 
bar and the three fixed tabs is consistent within the interactive prototype. As well, the design of 
the contained buttons and the outlined buttons is consistent. The last highlighted consistency 
within the prototype is the consistent titles e.g., below the tabs in the profile, which increase the 
scan-ability of the pages. Since all three consistencies are within the prototype, this is overall called 
internal consistency, which is included in the fourth heuristic Consistency and standards. Just like 
the external consistency, the internal consistency results in improved learnability and decreased 
cognitive load (Nielsen, 2020). 
Finally, the last page of the completed document involves two questions asked by the UX expert. 
A shortened version of these are seen below: 
1. Is it possible to create a shopping list based on previously scanned groceries? 
2. Is the user logged in since the welcome message on the home page includes a first name? 
 
6.4 Conclusion on Expert Review 
To conclude on the expert review, four usability problems are identified by the UX expert. The 
first usability problem in table 23 has received the highest severity rating, therefore this problem 
should be solved as the first. Based on the recommendations, the solution to this would be only to 
have a Save and Cancel button in the confirmation dialog appearing on the Nutritional info page. 
This confirmation dialog should appear when the users are leaving the page and have made 
changes to the main categories and or the types.  
==PAGE:133==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
129 
 
After this, the second usability problem in table 23 should be fixed since it has Medium as the 
severity rating. The UX expert has recommended several solutions to this problem and inspired by 
some of these, it should be made possible to save the favorite recipes. These could be placed in the 
bottom navigation bar with an icon and the text label “Recipes” instead of the search icon. This 
search icon doesn’t have any function and as well it confused the first and second participant in 
the thinking aloud tests since they thought that it could be used to identify groceries.  
In addition, the icons for the grocery effect category should be added on the groceries on the Latest 
scan page. The first reason being that the users shouldn’t remember the category since it isn’t 
compliant with the sixth usability heuristic Recognition rather than recall (Nielsen, 2020). The 
second reason is that the groceries on the Grocery effect page contain the icons, so in order to 
create internal consistency the icons should be added on the Latest scan page. This is done to be 
compliant with the fourth heuristic Consistency and standards (Nielsen, 2020). 
The last two usability problems to solve are the third and fourth in table 23, which are rated by the 
UX expert to have a low severity. Inspired by the recommendation for the third problem, 
consistency should be created for the button labels in the profile. Below the Glucose tab, the button 
text label for the blood glucose levels should be changed to “View”, while “Edit” should be the 
button text label for the blood glucose goals. As well, the third problem covers the inconsistent use 
of the Save and Cancel buttons. To fix this, the same confirmation dialog that should appear on 
the Nutritional info page could appear on the Blood glucose goals page when users have made and 
leave the page. Regarding the fourth usability problem, it should be solved by applying the 
recommendation from the UX expert, which is to remove the See all button on the Latest scans 
page and therefore to show all the latest scans on this page.  
Furthermore, the UX expert has found the three usability strengths in table 24. These should of 
course be kept in the interactive prototype. The second and third usability strength are about 
consistency covering the bottom navigation bar, the top area, the button design, and the titles on 
pages. In addition, the first usability strength is the feedback message appearing after changes on 
the Nutritional info page. This feedback message should as well be appearing on the Blood glucose 
goals page, and the feedback message should be shown as quickly as possible on both pages 
(Nielsen, 2020).  
==PAGE:134==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
130 
 
Finally, the UX expert has asked two questions. The answer to the first question is that shopping 
lists aren’t incorporated in the interactive prototype, because the POV in section 4.7 doesn’t 
include these. However, it may be useful for the users to add their previously scanned or searched 
groceries to shopping lists. Concerning the second question, the answer is that the scenario in the 
prototype is that the specific user has already logged in and registered her first name in the basic 
profile information. Therefore, this name can be incorporated in the welcome message on the home 
page.  
 
7. Discussion 
In the following section, the results of this project will be discussed by highlighting strengths and 
limitations. This involves the initial searches, the focus group, the co-design workshop, the 
prototyping, and the expert review. Lastly, the future steps will be covered. 
 
7.1 Initial searches 
As initial research, four series of searches were conducted, and the results were separated into six 
categories. The first category in Appendix 2 contains only 2 articles concerning habits of type 2 
diabetics only related to eating and not grocery shopping. The second category in Appendix 3 
includes only 7 articles describing challenges related to type 2 diabetes. This limited literature was 
an argument for performing the focus group to obtain more details about these topics (Halkier, 
2015). However, the final number of focus group participants ended up being only 2. These two 
participants haven’t most likely covered all the habits and challenges of type 2 diabetics. Especially 
since in the POV in section 4.7 is stated that type 2 diabetics are different. The representativity of 
the findings related to the topics could be improved by conducting a new round of literature search 
as well as another focus group involving more type 2 diabetics.  
The third category in Appendix 4 involves 11 articles about nudging and motivation interventions. 
In the preceding special course, 15 of such articles were identified. Therefore, the number of 
identified articles regarding this topic is fairly high. However, the manually created and colored 
blood glucose effect categories in original idea of the fourth participant were directly inspired by 
the warning labels described by Gupta et al. 2020. Afterwards, the second participant has 
==PAGE:135==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
131 
 
developed the idea further to automatically categories created based on continuous blood glucose 
measurement. This shows that nudging and motivation interventions are important inspiration for 
ideation, therefore another literature search could be performed before developing the fifth 
iteration of the prototype. 
The 61 identified mobile applications in Appendix 5 constitute the fourth category placed. In the 
preceding special course, 28 mobile applications were found. Therefore, many mobile applications 
are identified, and even more were found in Google Play, but these weren’t included due to similar 
functionalities as those already found. These functionalities were categorized into the 
characteristics seen in table 3. The limitations of these characteristics are that they don’t cover all 
identified functionalities and that the categorization is subjective since it’s only based on my 
assessment. Regarding technologies, searches weren’t performed in this project like in the special 
course. Such searches could be relevant in order to identify devices that can supplement the mobile 
application MyGlucoseShopping. The digital library of Association for Computing Machinery 
(ACM) and the search engine Google Scholar could be used for searching.  
The fifth category in Appendix 6 from the searches involves 3 articles about design and use of 
technologies. In the special course, a similar category contains 10 articles. Out of these 13 articles, 
only the article by Jain et al. 2020 describes technology preferences of type 2 diabetics. These 
preferences could therefore be further investigated by conducting literature searches in the 
database PubMed or by including these in another focus group.  
Finally, the sixth category of literature in Appendix 7 is Related Concepts containing 12 articles. 
10 of these were identified by only conducting two literature searches for resp. empowerment and 
technology literacy, and through these the concept health literacy and eHealth literacy were found. 
This showed that the concepts are closely connected, and a search for health literacy wasn’t 
therefore necessary. However, the concept technology literacy wasn’t identified as a concept but 
only as a challenge, therefore another search for this might be relevant. 
 
7.2 Focus group  
After the initial searches, the first Design Thinking phase was Empathize completed (The 
Interaction Design Foundation, n.d.). In this a focus group with only two type 2 diabetics was 
==PAGE:136==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
132 
 
carried out. This was despite that the selected and invited number of participants was 6, which was 
decided based on three factors (Krueger & Casey, 2014). The focus group with only two 
participants doesn’t qualify to be even a small focus group containing between 4 and 6 participants. 
One limitation of the performed focus group is therefore that it has covered very few grocery 
shopping habits and challenges of type 2 diabetics and as well their opinions related to a technology 
promoting healthy grocery shopping habits. Other type 2 diabetics could have other experiences 
and opinions. All the type 2 diabetics who submitted the form on the recruitment page should have 
been invited to the focus group since this potentially could have resulted in the small focus group 
with minimum 4 participants (Krueger & Casey, 2014).  
Another limitation related to the focus group is that the two focus group participants seemed to be 
too heterogenous since they struggled to understand each other (Halkier, 2015). An example of 
this is that the first participant suggested a list containing foods that aren’t diabetes-friendly. He 
would like to have this as a reminder, which relates to his need for guidance. Opposite, the second 
participant finds guidance and thereby the list unnecessary and provocative, because she has been 
on the strict LCHF diet for many years. Instead, she needs motivation.  
This heterogeneity would probably have been reduced if the focus group had involved more 
participants. However, the age and gender diversity would still have been limited since this 
diversity among the type 2 diabetics who submitted the recruitment form isn’t great. The number 
of males is only 4 out of 11. Regarding the age diversity, 7 out of the 9 type 2 diabetics who stated 
their age are in their 50s. Another factor, which could have reduced the heterogeneity, could have 
been a higher level of moderator involvement e.g., through an exercise. The two focus group 
participants often ended talking about topics not relevant for the theme. For example, this 
happened after the first open start-question concerning grocery shopping habits (Halkier, 2015). 
As a moderator, it was difficult to interrupt and end the discussion, therefore an exercise about 
these habits might have helped controlling the discussion. 
In the second Design Thinking phase, Define, the Abductive Grounded Theory process was 
performed, where the transcribed focus group was coded (The Interaction Design Foundation, 
n.d.)(Rahmani & Leifels, 2018). First of all, the focus group was difficult to transcribe since the 
data is based on social interactions (Halkier, 2015). It might therefore be misunderstood what the 
participants were saying especially when they were talking at the same time. Based on the 
==PAGE:137==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
133 
 
transcribed interactions, the Open coding and then Axial coding added another layer of subjectivity 
since the categories, subcategories, and open codes are made only on the basis of my interpretation 
of the interactions (Rahmani & Leifels, 2018). In addition, some of the subcategories below the 
categories of functionalities contain very few open codes. The reason being that the interactions 
related to the functionalities were limited since the two focus group participants categorized them 
individually in the second exercise. For example, the functionality Meal plans placed in the 
medium relevance category isn’t at all coded since it wasn’t mentioned by the participants.  
Furthermore, some of the functionalities that are coded and placed in the category Functionalities 
maybe to include in a technology could potentially solve some of identified challenges from 
Abductive Grounded Theory process (Rahmani & Leifels, 2018). The second, third, and fourth 
challenge concerning limitations of Diabetesforeningen below the category Challenges for type 2 
diabetics could for example be solved by the Social networking functionality. This could be online 
forum where type 2 diabetics can interact with each other and people from Diabetesforeningen 
about type 2 diabetes management. However, the creation of this online forum wasn’t possible 
within the scope of this project. In addition, the functionality Discounts and coupons included in 
the second focus group exercise could be a solution to the challenge Expensive diabetes-friendly 
foods and medicine of the Grocery shopping challenges. Despite of this, the functionality isn’t a 
result of the Abductive Grounded Theory process since the participants didn’t discuss it and as 
well they categorized it as not relevant. 
 
7.3 Co-design workshop 
After the focus group analysis, the co-design workshop including the third phase Ideate, the fourth 
phase Prototype, and the fifth phase Test was conducted (The Interaction Design Foundation, n.d.). 
The ideation and prototyping was involved in the first activity, brainwalking. This method was 
chosen instead of a classic brainstorming, because it ensures that all participants are engaged and 
contribute with ideas. As well, it encourages creativity and utilizes the knowledge of the 
participants when they are further developing others’ ideas (Københavns Universitet, n.d.-a). 
Therefore, the ideas are created through collaboration. Opposite, in the classical brainstorming 
each participant is the owner of their own ideas, which can cause limited creativity due to self-
censorship (Københavns Universitet, n.d.-b).  
==PAGE:138==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
134 
 
However, there are some limitations of the performed brainwalking. First of all, the third 
participant misunderstood the outcome of the first round, which should be one idea including a 
name, a description, and a sketch (Københavns Universitet, n.d.-a). Instead, he has generated three 
original ideas and hasn’t included a sketch. As well, a sketch isn’t included in original idea by the 
first participant. During the instructions, it should probably have been described more clearly how 
to create the original idea. Likewise, the design concept creation in the third round of the 
brainwalking should have been explained more in details. The reason being that the third 
participant didn’t create his design concept based on any of his three original ideas or the ideas 
added by the other participants in the second round.  
In the second round, the first participant didn’t add any ideas for further development of the second 
participant’s original idea. Instead, he just stated his support of this idea, which could indicate that 
he was inspired by it. One reason for this could be that both the first and second participant’s 
original idea were based on their discussions about functionalities in the focus group. These 
functionalities were inspired by most of the characteristics in table 3, which are present in mobile 
applications. In addition, this inspiration could have biased the two participants to create a mobile 
application as their design concepts. 
Furthermore, another iteration of the affinity diagramming of the design concepts should have 
been performed because some of the categories are overlapping. This applies for Profile and Blood 
sugar levels since the subcategory Health data below Profile contains blood sugar levels, and the 
Goals subcategory below Profile involves goals related to blood sugar. This could be solved by 
moving Goals and the blood sugar related below Health data to the category Blood sugar levels. 
In this category, it could be mentioned if anything should appear in the profile. In addition, the 
category Scanning functionality as well indicates the need for another iteration of the affinity 
diagramming since it contains two different functionalities. In this case, the solution could be to 
split the category into the scanning itself and the scanning results. 
In the co-design workshop, the second activity was the thinking aloud tests, which constituted the 
Test phase. The overall limitation of this activity is that only three tests were performed. For 
usability tests, the recommended number of participants is five since this would result in almost 
the same number of usability problems as if more participants are included (Nielsen, 2012b). 
Another limitation related to the performed tests is that I as a facilitator might have biased some 
==PAGE:139==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
135 
 
of the participants. Especially, a potential bias is my explanation of and assistance regarding the 
scanning field when the first participant tried to solve the fourth task (Nielsen, 2012a).  
Regarding this scanning field in the prototype of the mobile application YourHealthyShopping, 
the weakness is that it didn’t include an image of a barcode. This might have caused that the first 
participant didn’t understand the usage of the field, therefore it took him several tries to solve the 
task being marked with a red heart. Therefore, this missing image could have resulted in a 
misleading severity rating. Overall, the identification and severity rating of usability strengths and 
problems based on the findings from the thinking aloud tests were only on the basis of my 
knowledge and experience. Therefore, another researcher could have identified other usability 
strengths and problems and as well rated them differently (Budiu, 2017).  
A last limitation related to the thinking aloud tests is that the first and second participant could 
have felt highly involved in the project since they as well participated in the focus group. This 
could have resulted in that they didn’t mention all their criticism related to the tested. An example 
is that they didn’t comment on the personal discounts despite that they both categorized this 
functionality as not of relevance in the second focus group exercise. As well, they didn’t include 
the functionality in their design concepts.  
 
7.4 Prototyping 
For the reasons just mentioned, the personal discounts are removed in the fourth iteration of the 
prototype. Opposite, other functionalities are added in the iteration such as healthier alternatives 
and recipes, which appear in a scanning and search result. As a result of the Abductive Grounded 
Theory process, both the functionality Recommendations and suggestions and the functionality 
Shopping lists and recipes were categorized as some maybe to include. The argument for including 
healthier alternatives was that these are included in half of the design concepts, but possibly this 
argument isn’t strong enough since I as a designer made one of the design concepts. For including 
the recipes in the scanning and search result, the same argument was used. However, the recipes 
aren’t included as a part of the scanning and search result. Instead, this was an idea from my end 
on basis of the second participant’s idea concerning that the recipes should be based on scanned 
groceries. In addition, the recipes are included despite that Collection of recipes is found to be the 
fourth most common characteristic in the identified mobile application in table 3.  
==PAGE:140==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
136 
 
Another functionality added in the fourth iteration is the blood glucose levels. These levels are 
visualized on a page via graphs and numbers. A requirement for these visualizations is a CGM 
system since blood glucose data will be transferred from a mobile application in such a system. 
Likewise, food diary data from the mobile application is a requirement for Grocery effect page 
separating groceries into three colored blood glucose effect categories (Veri, n.d.). The fact that a 
CGM system is required for these functionalities can be problematic since the accessibility is poor. 
Type 2 diabetics need to procure a CGM from outside Denmark and pay for it themselves (Persson 
et al., n.d.). Some can possibly not afford such a system. One solution to this could be to provide 
a free CGM to newly diagnosed type 2 diabetics, which was a suggestion by the second participant 
in the focus group.  
In addition, the POV in section 4.7 states that type 2 diabetics are different e.g., regarding their use 
of technology. Some type 2 diabetics are measuring their blood glucose via a CGM system like 
the second focus group participant, whereas other type 2 diabetics may rarely measure their blood 
glucose (Persson et al., n.d.). The first focus group participant mentioned that he measures when 
he initiates a new diet. Therefore, the mobile application MyGlucoseShopping should be possible 
to use without a CGM system. In this case, the functionalities requiring a CGM system could be 
empty pages in the mobile application. 
The blood glucose goals are as well requiring a GMS since these goals are suggested on the basis 
of blood glucose data. These goals are made editable for the type 2 diabetics. However, this may 
not be ethical since some type 2 diabetics especially newly diagnosed might not know anything 
about blood glucose. Another possible ethical problem in the fourth iteration is the recommended 
maximum intake appearing in a scanning and search result. Some type 2 diabetics could feel that 
this is provocative since they already are aware of this. As well, it should be investigated if this 
calculation of tolerated amount at all is technical possible. Likewise, it should be investigated how 
to ensure that detailed nutritional information about types of sweeteners can be obtained. First of 
all, this would probably require changes to the requirements concerning food labeling. 
 
7.5 Expert Review 
In a review of the fourth iteration, an UX expert has identified four usability problems. The next 
iteration of the prototype being the fifth iteration should be created based on the concluded 
==PAGE:141==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
137 
 
improvements in section 6.4. Afterwards, this prototype should be usability tested with minimum 
five other type 2 diabetics (Nielsen, 2012b). This usability test could for example be another 
thinking aloud test with the purpose to identify strengths and problems in the updated design 
(Nielsen, 2012a)(Budiu, 2017). Additional to a usability test, more reviews could be performed 
with other UX experts (Harley, 2018). The number of reviewers should be more than one since the 
severity rating of usability problems by only one reviewer isn’t trustworthy (Nielsen, 1994).  
In addition, the UX expert who reviewed the fourth iteration of the prototype only used the ten 
usability heuristics for interaction design (Nielsen, 2020). By including other UX experts, their 
evaluations would probably include other usability guidelines, principles, and research (Harley, 
2018). Furthermore, experts with knowledge of type 2 diabetes could as well assess the fifth 
iteration of the prototype in order to identify benefits and critical elements related to the disease. 
Overall, additional iterations of the Design Thinking phases are necessary to ensure that the POV 
describes a central challenge for type 2 diabetics and that MyGlucoseShopping therefore promotes 
healthy grocery shopping habits in supermarkets for type 2 diabetics. 
 
8. Conclusion  
Initially, the four series of searches in this project have resulted in the identification of resp. 2 and 
7 articles concerning habits and challenges of type 2 diabetics. As well, 61 existing mobile 
applications promoting healthy grocery shopping and 4 related concepts are identified. In addition, 
the searches have resulted in 11 articles about nudging and motivation interventions and 3 articles 
concerning design and use of technologies. Afterwards, a focus group with two type 2 diabetics 
was performed in the first phase Emphasize of the Design Thinking process, and in the following 
Define phase, the focus group data was analyzed using the Abductive Grounded Theory process. 
These findings are related to the identified literature in this project or the preceding special course 
as well as the characteristics of functionalities. The result of the Define phase is a POV highlighting 
that type 2 diabetics are different and responsible for their own health. As well, it consists of three 
needs for resp. personalization, nutritional information, and knowledge of how different groceries 
affect the blood glucose.  
==PAGE:142==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
138 
 
The following three phases Ideate, Prototype, and Test involved a co-design workshop with three 
type 2 diabetics and one designer. Through a brainwalking, the ideation and prototyping occurred, 
while three thinking aloud tests constituted the testing. Based on the results of these activities and 
the prototype from the preceding special course, another prototyping was performed resulting in 
an interactive prototype of the mobile application MyGlucoseShopping, which is designed to solve 
the needs described in the POV. Afterwards, this prototype was reviewed by an UX expert that 
constituted another testing and has resulted in four identified usability problems. These should be 
corrected in another iteration, which then should be tested. Overall, more iterations the Design 
Thinking phases are needed both validate the prototype and the POV, so it’s ensured that 
MyGlucoseShopping is a of a micro-intervention technology promoting healthy grocery shopping 
habits in supermarkets for type 2 diabetics.

Appendix 1 – Search Protocol 
 
Search protocol 
Research questions 
1. Which grocery shopping habits do type 2 diabetics have? 
2. Which challenges do type 2 diabetics experience related to 
healthy grocery shopping? 
3. What mobile applications exist to promote healthy grocery 
shopping in supermarkets? 
4. What do the related concepts empowerment, health literacy, and 
technology literacy cover? 
Literature sources 
The first and second research question will be investigated with 
searches with the search engine Google Scholar and in the database 
PubMed. Google Scholar gives diverse results, while PubMed results 
in articles within health science.  
 
The third research question requires an investigation using the search 
engine Google, the website Apple, the online store Google Play, and 
the digital library of ACM. Google results in the most popular mobile 
applications, while ACM could result in unpublished applications. The 
Apple website contains iOS applications, while Google Play contains 
applications for Android. 
 
The fourth research question will be answered by searches within the 
database PubMed. This database will contain an explanation of the 
concepts within health science. 
Search strategies 
Regarding the first research question, a block search will be performed 
in PubMed using MeSH-terms and free text. Some free text will be 
truncated. With Google Scholar, searches (both block and standard) 
conducted.  
 
Regarding the second research question, block searches will be 
conducted in PubMed using MeSH-terms and truncated free text. 
Afterwards, a standard search will be performed in Google Scholar.  
 
Regarding the third research question, standard searches will be 
performed using all the literature sources (Google, Apple, Google 
Play, and ACM).  
Regarding the fourth research question, block searches will be 
conducted in PubMed with MeSH-terms and free text. The free text 
will be truncated, where it makes sense. 
Inclusion criteria 
• 
The first 30-40 search results due to most relevant.  
• 
English as the language (not for third research question). 
• 
Systematic reviews 
- 
Used for fourth and fifth search related to the first research 
question to obtain common challenges for type 2 diabetics. 
==PAGE:156==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
152 
 
- 
Used for the first search related to the fourth research 
question to get overall descriptions of empowerment.  
Exclusion criteria 
• 
Articles older than 10 years (used in some of the searches related 
to the first and second research question to get the present habits 
and challenges). 
• 
Articles identified in the preceding special course. 
• 
Articles identified in previous searches in this project. 
• 
Mobile applications concerning blood sugar monitoring, activity 
tracking, or online grocery shopping. 
• 
Mobile applications for supermarkets in other countries than 
Denmark. 
• 
Mobile applications constituting a game. 
 
 
==PAGE:157==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
153 
 
Appendix 2 – Habits of type 2 diabetics 
 
Article 
Habits 
(Burch et al., 2018) 
• 
Systematic review to compare the dietary intake of 
type 2 diabetics with national and WHO’s 
guidelines on diet.  
• 
The consumption of fruit, vegetables, grains, and 
dairy was less than the recommendations for most 
of the type 2 diabetics participating.  
(Savoca & Miller, 2001) 
• 
The knowledge related to diabetes management 
influence type 2 diabetics eating habits.  
• 
As well, social support and time management are 
influencing their eating habits. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:158==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
154 
 
Appendix 3 – Challenges of type 2 diabetics  
 
Article 
Challenges 
(Alvarado et al., 2017) 
• 
Systematic review of challenges regarding remote 
health interventions to support type 2 diabetes self-
management. 
• 
Technology illiteracy was one of the most common 
challenges related to the interventions. 
(Breuing et al., 2020) 
• 
Systematic review of challenges related to prevention 
of type 2 diabetes and gestational diabetes. 
• 
Limited knowledge, family/friends, and economic 
factors were identified as the most common 
challenges. 
(Campos et al., 2011) 
• 
Systematic review of use and understanding of 
nutrition labels on pre-packaged foods. 
• 
Nutrition labels are used by many customers as the 
guide to select foods. 
• 
The use of nutrition labels are evidentially connected 
to healthier diets. 
• 
Understanding and using nutrition labels can be a 
challenge to some customers. 
• 
It’s important to ensure that the information on 
nutrition labels is understandable. 
(Gardsten et al., 2018) 
• 
Investigation of challenges regarding type 2 diabetes 
self-management in everyday life.  
• 
Two focus group interviews, where one was with 
recently diagnosed type 2 diabetics and the other with 
diabetics with longer experience. 
• 
Choosing healthy foods and eating regularly were 
challenges for both groups.  
(Jain et al., 2020) 
• 
Poor technology literacy and poor literacy are 
identified as challenges for self-management of type 2 
diabetes. 
(Stotz et al., 2021) 
• 
The first identified challenge for type 2 diabetes self-
management is insufficient existing food and nutrition 
resources.  
• 
The second challenge identified is a poor 
socioeconomic status. 
(Wilkinson et al., 2014) 
• 
Systematic review of challenges for self-management 
of type 1 or 2 diabetes. 
• 
Communication, education, personal factors, provider 
issues, and support are the main challenges. 
• 
Regarding communication, the communication with 
health care providers seems to be challenging. 
 
==PAGE:159==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
155 
 
Appendix 4 – Nudging and Motivation interventions 
 
Article 
Nudging and motivation interventions 
(Au et al., 2013) 
• 
Analysis of the cost-effectiveness of nudging 
interventions with the purpose to promote healthy 
diets for overweight and obese adult women. 
• 
A predetermined grocery shopping list showed to 
cost-effective and to be more effective and cost 
saving than a standard behavioral therapy.  
(DiCosola III & Neff, 2020) 
• 
Pilot study of how to nudge customers to make 
healthier grocery purchases online. 
• 
The intervention included social comparisons of 
online grocery baskets. 
• 
When compared to customers interested in new 
diets, consumers not interested are motivated to 
reduce calorie intake. 
• 
Customers interested in new diets are motivated to 
reduce calorie intake regardless of social 
comparison.  
(Gittelsohn et al., 2017) 
• 
Systematic review of food-pricing interventions to 
promote healthy foods/beverages. 
• 
Increased purchase and consumption of healthy 
foods/beverages or decreased purchase and 
consumption of unhealthy foods/beverages were 
found in most studies.  
(Gupta et al., 2020) 
• 
Systematic review of nudging interventions with 
the purpose to reduce the purchase of sugar-
sweetened beverages. 
• 
The interventions included either front of pack 
labels or point of sale signage, which highlighted 
the sugar content and the health consequences 
related to diet. 
• 
Improved health knowledge and attitudes 
concerning the beverages as well as reduced 
purchase of them were the results of the 
interventions. 
• 
Especially, warning labels with health 
consequences related to diet had an effect. 
(Li et al., 2009) 
• 
A suggested recommender system for grocery 
shopping performing better than other systems.  
• 
The system involves:  
1) A measure for product similarity. 
2) A model to estimate product similarity. 
3) Online adjustment of recommendations 
according to the current shopping basket. 
==PAGE:160==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
156 
 
4) A new measure for products not usually or 
never purchased by customers. 
(S. G. Moore et al., 2018) 
• 
Systematic review of educational interventions 
related to nutrition labels. 
• 
Some studies included diabetics. 
• 
The educational interventions were taught sessions 
and web-based learning. 
• 
These interventions lead to improved 
understanding or use of nutrition labels. 
• 
Health literacy and nutrition knowledge should be 
further investigated in research. 
(Spencer-Bonilla et al., 2017) 
• 
Systematic review of randomized conical trials 
(RCTs) concerning social network interventions 
for type 2 diabetes self-management. 
• 
The interventions are often educational and 
engaging the social network to involve their 
knowledge and experience.  
• 
Improved social support is a result of the social 
network interventions.  
(Stotz et al., 2021) 
• 
For type 2 diabetics, motivating interventions for 
healthy eating, which are taking the 
socioeconomic status into account, are nutritional 
counseling, cooking classes, and support groups. 
• 
Resources to access healthy foods is another 
motivating intervention for the 8 type diabetics, 
who were included in the focus group conducted. 
(von Philipsborn et al., 2020) 
• 
Systematic review of nudging interventions with 
the purpose to reduce the intake of sugar-
sweetened beverages. 
• 
Traffic light labelling and price increases were 
some of the interventions, which resulted in a 
decreased intake of sugar-sweetened beverages. 
(Wan et al., 2017) 
• 
Study investigating how to create a recommender 
system for online and offline shoppers including 
both personal preferences and price sensitivities.  
• 
The suggested scalable framework can provide 
satisfying recommendations by being personalized 
and by obtaining information about price 
sensitivity of customers. 
(Wayman & Madhvanath, 2015) 
• 
Research project, Foodle, with the purpose to 
nudge customers to make healthier grocery 
purchases. 
• 
Automated dietary guidance adjusted to individual 
goals.  
• 
The guidance is based on data from digital receipts 
for grocery purchases in supermarkets.  
 
==PAGE:161==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021

Appendix 6 – Design and Use of Technologies 
 
Article 
Design and use of technologies 
(Baldwin et al., 2017) 
• 
Health information technology has the potential to 
increase health information access, however this 
information can be difficult to understand and 
navigate in if the interface is small and complex.  
• 
Discussion of two types of health information 
technologies, patient portals and mobile health 
applications.  
• 
Patient portals are difficult to use due to poor 
design and issues related to health literacy. 
• 
Mobile health applications are more intuitive and 
easier to use compared to patient portals due to 
user-friendly features e.g., real-time tracking and 
simple data display.  
• 
The suggestion is to improve user experience of 
patient portals by adopting design, function, and 
interface of mobile health applications. 
• 
A combination of mobile applications and patient 
portals could lead to increased patient engagement 
and self-management and thereby empowerment 
of patients.  
(Jain et al., 2020) 
• 
Type 2 diabetics prefer technologies, which are 
easy in regards to access, use, and apply.  
• 
As well, it’s important to them that technologies 
contain reliable information. 
• 
Lastly, they appreciate support from peers.  
(O’Leary et al., 2015) 
• 
To design tailored self-management interventions, 
it’s important to understand the attitudes of 
patients with chronic conditions.  
• 
Analysis of patients’ attitudes toward use of 
technology and involvement in their care.  
• 
Three clusters of patients are found being 
“Proactive Techies”, “Indie Self-Managers”, and 
“Remind me! Non Techies”.  
• 
These clusters aren’t affected by race, age, and 
education level.  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:177==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
173 
 
Appendix 7 – Related Concepts 
 
Article 
Related concepts 
(Baldoni et al., 2017) 
• 
Systematic review and meta-analysis to investigate 
collective empowerment strategies for diabetes 
patients. 
• 
These strategies improve clinical parameters, 
behavior, and self-care as well as increase 
knowledge about diabetes. 
(Bomfim et al., 2020) 
• 
Field-study of a mobile application (Pirate Bri’s 
Grocery Adventure) with the purpose to improve 
food literacy when grocery shopping. 
• 
Food literacy is defined as “(…) the 
interconnected combination of food-related 
knowledge, skills, and behaviors that empower an 
individual to make informed food choices (…)”  
(Bomfim & Wallace, 2018) 
• 
Design of a mobile application (Pirate Bri’s 
Grocery Adventure) with the purpose to improve 
food literacy when grocery shopping. 
• 
Food literacy is connected to “(…) the 
improvement of autonomy and confidence around 
food, healthier dietary intake, and chronic disease 
prevention.” 
(Cherid et al., 2020) 
• 
Knowledge of health and eHealth literacy level 
can support the development of more effective 
eHealth interventions.  
• 
A survey among older adults in Canada with a 
recent fracture is conducted to investigate the 
technology use as well as the health literacy and 
eHealth literacy level. 
• 
The survey included self-assessments via the 8-
item eHealth literacy scale (eHEALS). 
• 
Many of the older adults use mobile technology, 
and their health and eHealth literacy show that it’s 
feasible to develop eHealth interventions to them.  
• 
High health literacy is associated with younger age 
and higher education.  
(Dunn & Hazzard, 2019) 
• 
Digital health literacy has the same definition as 
health literacy just in the context of health 
technology use. 
• 
Health literacy can be promoted with health 
technology by improving the functional and 
critical skills within in the healthcare system e.g., 
navigation, communication, and shared decision 
making.  
==PAGE:178==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
174 
 
(Fernández-Gutiérrez et al., 2018) 
• 
Systematic review of the effect of health literacy 
interventions for immigrant populations. 
• 
“Health literacy is a social health determinant that 
influences improvement in health, patient 
empowerment and reduction in inequalities.” 
• 
Health literacy includes different dimensions being 
functional (reading, writing, and arithmetic skills), 
interactive (social and cognitive skills), and critical 
(skills to analyze information and to make 
informed decisions).  
• 
The interventions identified seem to mainly 
improve the functional health literacy.  
(S. H. Kim & Lee, 2016) 
• 
Systematic review and meta-analysis to investigate 
the effect of diabetes management interventions 
sensitive to health literacy. 
• 
The interventions identified included four domains 
being written communication, spoken 
communication, empowerment, and 
language/cultural consideration. 
• 
Results of the interventions are improved 
cognitive skills, self-care, and health outcomes. 
• 
Low health literacy could be a challenge related to 
diabetes self-management; therefore, the domains 
should be implemented by healthcare providers. 
(H. Kim & Xie, 2017) 
• 
Systematic review to find literature regarding 
people with low health literacy and their use of 
online health services, which will lead to insights 
of how health literacy should be approached in the 
era of eHealth. 
• 
Challenges related to eHealth service use can be 
poor content readability and poor usability.  
• 
Mobile applications are a great opportunity to 
provide eHealth services to people with low health 
literacy.  
(Norman & Skinner, 2006) 
• 
Introduction to a model of eHealth literacy.  
• 
eHealth literacy is “(…) defined as the ability to 
seek, find, understand, and appraise health 
information from electronic sources and apply the 
knowledge gained to addressing or solving a 
health problem.” 
• 
The model introduced is called the eHealth literacy 
lily model, which contains multiple literacy types 
being health literacy, traditional literacy and 
numeracy, computer literacy, media literacy, 
science literacy as well as information literacy.  
==PAGE:179==
Kristine Amalie Ohlen 
Master Thesis 
20th December 2021 
175 
 
(Shiferaw et al., 2020) 
• 
The internet is used by chronic patients to find 
different health information e.g., about nutrition 
and disease management. 
• 
This requires the eHealth literacy skill, which is 
defined as the“(…) ability to search, find, 
appraise, and use health information from the 
internet (…)”  
• 
A cross-sectional survey among chronic patients in 
North-west Ethiopia is conducted to investigate 
the eHealth literacy level and associated factors 
with this skills.  
• 
The survey included a self-assessment of eHealth 
literacy via the eHealth literacy scale (eHEALS), 
which is an 8-item Likert scale.  
• 
The eHealth literacy level among chronic patients 
in the investigated location is relatively low.  
• 
High eHealth literacy is found to be positively 
associated with higher education, higher income, 
urban resident, and knowledge about online 
resources. 
(Vaitkeviciute et al., 2015) 
• 
Systematic review to investigate the association 
between the concept food literacy and dietary 
intake among adults. 
• 
A positive association between food literacy and 
dietary intake (great food knowledge and healthy 
dietary intake) was found in most of the identified 
studies (8).  
• 
Some studies (3) found a mixed association, and 
few studies (2) no association between food 
literacy and dietary intake.  
(Werbrouck et al., 2018) 
• 
Systematic review and meta-analysis to investigate 
the effectiveness of interventions related to patient 
empowerment. 
• 
Patient empowerment is “a concept focused on 
patient-centeredness and patients autonomy (…)” 
• 
Successful interventions are empowering patients 
through goal setting and action planning. 
• 
Knowledge could be used for empowerment 
together with goal setting and action planning.
